201 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


McAlister v. City of Fairway


No. 99,808, No. 99,809


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 391; 212 P.3d 184; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 398



July 24, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Johnson district court; GERALD T. ELLIOTT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens proposed city ordinances through the initiative and referendum process to place certain restrictions on the City's ability to relocate its city hall facilities and its use of rezoning, eminent domain, and condemnation. Appellee City refused to adopt the ordinances or advance them for public vote. The Johnson District Court, Kansas, ruled in favor of the City and granted its motion for summary judgment. The citizens appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the citizens contended the district court erred in ruling in favor of the City. The supreme court disagreed, finding that the first petition was principally executive or administrative in nature as its prohibition of relocating the city hall facilities was so extensive that it made unavailable more than 90 percent of the City's geographic territory. This had the practical effect of dictating where the City located its city hall facilities. Such a restriction necessarily limited and intruded to a substantial extent into areas of city administration requiring specialized knowledge about city affairs, regulatory requirements, long-range planning, and financing. These were all subject matters typically left to city administrators. The second petition was also administrative and thus excluded under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-3013, as it required the City to take action to not allow other entities to invoke statutory authority granted them by the legislature, which were matters of statewide concern (impact rezoning and eminent domain authority). Thus, the City's refusal to adopt the ordinances or advance them for public vote was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

202 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Richmond


No. 100,074


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 419; 212 P.3d 165; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 402



July 24, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Richmond v. State, 287 P.3d 299, 2012 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 926 (Kan. Ct. App., 2012)
Post-conviction relief denied at Richmond v. State, 2016 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 927 (Kan. Ct. App., Nov. 23, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Crawford district court, DONALD R. NOLAND, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Crawford District Court (Kansas) that convicted him of first-degree premeditated murder, arguing that the trial court erred in admitting a statement made by defendant more than two years before the shooting and erred in permitting the admission of specific instances of defendant's drug dealing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first-degree murder after killing a well-known drug dealer. At trial, a drug task force agent testified that she interviewed defendant earlier as part of a cocaine investigation and defendant told her that he did not sell crack cocaine but that his speciality was robbing and killing people. On review, the court held that defendant's statement was properly admitted under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455 because the statement essentially referred to defendant's sense of identity and did not refer to a specific crime or civil wrong. Further, the trial court did not err in admitting evidence of specific instances of defendant's drug dealing under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455 because the evidence was relevant to establish defendant's knowledge of the value of drugs and defendant's awareness of the amount of money that drug dealers were known to carry. The evidence was also relevant to establish motive and defendant's knowledge of the local drug scene. Any harm arising from this evidence was tempered by a jury instruction that informed jurors that they could only consider the specific crimes evidence to explain defendant's motive and his knowledge of the drug culture.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

 

203 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Salas


No. 99,830


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 245; 210 P.3d 635; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 338



July 10, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court; CLARK V. OWENS II, judge.
State v. Salas, 45 P.3d 403, 2002 Kan. App. LEXIS 440 (Kan. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Sedgwick District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of intentional second-degree murder and criminal possession of a firearm related to the death of the victim. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions. Defendant's motion for DNA testing under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2512 was denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant conceded that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2512 did not provide him a right to DNA testing and focused on an equal protection argument by comparing the similarity between premeditated first-degree murder and intentional second-degree murder. The supreme court found that the crimes of premeditated first-degree murder and intentional second-degree murder were distinguished by the premeditation element. The supreme court had characterized premeditation as the defining element of premeditated first-degree murder in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(a). In contrast, intentional second-degree murder had no requirement that the defendant think the matter over beforehand. Defendant failed to meet his burden of establishing that he was similarly situated to those who had a right to DNA testing under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2512, and his equal protection argument failed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

204 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Henning


Nos. 98,118, 98,119


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 136; 209 P.3d 711; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 174



June 26, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the Court of Appeals in State v. Henning, 38 Kan. App. 2d 706, 171 P.3d 660 (2007). Appeal from Lyon district court; MERLIN G. WHEELER, judge.
State v. Henning, 38 Kan. App. 2d 706, 171 P.3d 660, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 1150 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing and remanding to the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In each defendants' cases, the district court held that a police officer's search was unconstitutional. After the Court of Appeals (Kansas) reversed and remanded, the court granted defendants' petition for review.


OVERVIEW: A police officer saw one defendant walk thought a parking lot and get into the passenger side of a car. After verifying that there was a warrant for such defendant, he asked defendant step out of the car. The second defendant was sitting in the driver's set. The first defendant was arrested. The officer then searched the car while both defendants stood on a sidewalk near the car. In the car's closed center console, the officer found a items containing drug residue. On appeal, the court determined that Arizona v. Gant controlled and that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-2501(c) was unconstitutional. The court determined that the current wording of § 22-2501(c) would permit a search of a vehicle incident to an occupant's or a recent occupant's arrest, even if the purpose of the search was not focused on uncovering evidence only of the crime of arrest. Section 22-2501(c) was thus facially unconstitutional under the U.S. Const. amend. IV and under Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15. Accordingly, the search violated defendants' rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court and reversed the appellate court.

 

205 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Casady


No. 99,023


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 150; 210 P.3d 113; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 181



June 26, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Casady, 40 Kan. App. 2d 335, 191 P.3d 1130, 2008 Kan. App. LEXIS 139 (2008)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to the count of possession of prescription drugs without a prescription. The district court sentenced her to 30 months' incarceration. At sentencing, the district court also assessed court costs of $ 163, a drug testing fee of $ 10, a booking fee of $ 25, and a Board of Indigents' Defense Services (BIDS) fee of $ 100. Defendant appealed the BIDS fee. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed; defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4529, which provided for payment of a $ 100 application fee by indigent defendants seeking the appointment of counsel from BIDS. The court held that the statute was constitutional because safeguards protected an indigent defendant's access to counsel, including a provision for the district court to reduce or waive a previously imposed application fee. The district court was required to determine the propriety of imposing the Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4529 application fee at the time of the initial determination to appoint counsel; however, any enforcement of the obligation to pay the fee was to be deferred until the conclusion of proceedings in district court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Court of Appeals, holding that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4529 was constitutional.

 

206 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Richardson


No. 100,445, No. 100,835


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 118; 209 P.3d 696; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 180



June 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Richardson, 218 P.3d 1197, 2009 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 941 (Kan. Ct. App., 2009)
Related proceeding at State v. Richardson, 2011 Kan. App. LEXIS 154 (Kan. Ct. App., Nov. 4, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Lyon district court; JEFFRY J. LARSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Lyon District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of two counts of exposing another to a life-threatening communicable disease, namely, human immunodeficiency virus (HIV), in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3435. Defendant was sentenced to consecutive prison terms, and he appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the statute defining the crime, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3435, was unconstitutionally vague; the district court erred in failing to treat § 21-3435 as a specific intent crime; and the evidence was insufficient to convict him of violating § 21-3435. The supreme court held that the State was required to prove that defendant, knowing he was infected with HIV, intentionally engaged in sexual intercourse with the victims with the specific intent to expose them to HIV. It further held that a person of ordinary intelligence would understand what § 21-3435 meant by the term "life threatening; the statute conveyed sufficient definite warning to the citizenry as to the conduct which was proscribed and provided explicit standards for those that applied the law. As such, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. However, the supreme court agreed with defendant that the State failed to prove circumstances from which a rational factfinder could reasonably have inferred that defendant had the specific intent to expose either victim to HIV.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

207 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Frick v. City of Salina


No. 99,791


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


289 Kan. 1; 208 P.3d 739; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 104



June 5, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Frick v. City of Salina, 290 Kan. 869, 235 P.3d 1211, 2010 Kan. LEXIS 432 (2010)
Appeal after remand at Frick v. City of Salina, 2011 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 959 (Kan. Ct. App., Nov. 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Saline district court; DAN D. BOYER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Saline District Court (Kansas) held that its scope of review of appellant property owners' appeal of an administrative action was limited. The district court affirmed the decision of the hearing examiner with respect to relocation costs appellee city owed to the property owners. The property owners appealed and filed a motion to transfer the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The city undertook a public improvement project that involved reconstruction of a street, the construction of a bridge, and the reconstruction of appurtenant side roads. The project required the city to acquire the real property of the property owners. The city advised the property owners of the general eligibility requirements and procedures for obtaining relocation benefits for the property owners' displaced businesses, in accordance with the Kansas Relocation Assistance for Persons Displaced by Acquisition of Real Property Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 58-3501 et seq. The property owners contended that when they appealed the administrative hearing examiner's decisions, they were entitled to receive a new trial on the merits. The supreme court concluded that the district court was correct to impose limitations to its review rather than grant an entirely new procedure, but the district court erred in applying the substantial competent evidence standard. In considering the appeal under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 58-3509(a), the district court should have made independent findings of fact and conclusions of law.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the district court's decision, and the matter was remanded for application of the proper standard of review.

 

208 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Youngblood


No. 96,850


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 659; 206 P.3d 518; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 87



May 8, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in No. 96,850, unpublished opinion filed April 25, 2008. Appeal from Harvey district court; JOE DICKINSON, judge.
State v. Youngblood, 2008 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 349 (2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed. Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Because of a prior misdemeanor possession of marijuana conviction, defendant was charged with possession of marijuana, a felony pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4162(a). Defendant was convicted and sentenced for possession of hallucinogenic drugs, as a second offense. The Kansas Court of Appeals held that the State failed to carry its burden of proving that defendant was advised of his right to counsel. Review was sought.


OVERVIEW: Sheriff officers arrested defendant for driving on a suspended license. At a detention center, defendant was caught trying to discard a pipe, which subsequently tested positive for marijuana. On appeal, defendant claimed that the prior misdemeanor conviction used to elevate the severity level of the offense from a misdemeanor to a felony was unconstitutionally obtained without counsel or a valid waiver of counsel. The State was required to show that defendant's initial waiver was properly obtained after appropriate advice as to the right to counsel, in other words, that the waiver was knowingly and intelligently made. The supreme court agreed with the intermediate appellate court that the State failed to carry its burden. Defendant was entitled to counsel when the municipal court found defendant guilty and sentenced defendant to a prison term, even though the jail time was a condition of probation. The denial of the right to counsel rendered defendant's uncounseled misdemeanor conviction unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. VI. The unconstitutional conviction could not be collaterally used in district court for sentence enhancement.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's holding. The supreme court reversed and remanded the matter.

 

209 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fischer


No. 100,334


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 470; 203 P.3d 1269; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 53



March 27, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Fischer, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 468 (Kan., Mar. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court; WARREN M. WILBERT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered a guilty plea to two felony charges. After being sentenced, by the Sedgwick District Court, Kansas, to a controlling prison term of 40 months, defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that without the inclusion of her juvenile adjudications, her criminal history score would have been different, and her presumptive sentence would have been 19 to 21 months' imprisonment. When defendant was adjudicated, she received all of the process she was due, and she was afforded all of the constitutional protections then required in such proceedings. Before defendant's juvenile adjudications were final, they were prior convictions under the Apprendi exception, and the district court properly included them in her criminal history scoring. Alternatively, defendant argued that the use of any of her prior convictions or adjudications to enhance her sentence violated her rights under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments. Essentially, defendant asked the appellate court to accept her prediction that the United States Supreme Court would modify its Apprendi holding to eliminate the express exception for prior convictions, i.e., that the court will someday require all sentence-enhancing facts, including prior convictions, to be pled and proved to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. The supreme court declined this invitation to prognosticate.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

210 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Martinez


No. 99,641


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 443; 204 P.3d 601; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 58



March 27, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Martinez, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 470 (Kan., Mar. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Wyandotte district court; JOHN J. McNALLY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) that convicted him of premeditated first-degree murder and criminal discharge of a firearm at an occupied vehicle, arguing that an ex parte conversation between a juror and the judge violated his right to be present all critical stages of trial under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3405(1) and that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct the jury on reckless second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: After the jury began deliberating, a juror approached the judge regarding the competency of another juror. The judge notified the parties of the conversation and held a hearing before directing the jury to continue its deliberations. On review, the court held that, although the trial judge erred in speaking with the juror without the presence of counsel and defendant, the error was harmless because the strength of the prosecution's case was great. Defendant was seen in a car near the shooting, he confessed and took sole responsibility for the shooting, and physical evidence corroborated his confession. The ex parte communication did not involve a critical aspect of the trial, and the trial judge mitigated any harm by conducting a hearing with counsel and defendant present before the jury resumed deliberations following the ex parte contact. Further, the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on reckless second-degree murder as a lesser included offense of premeditated first-degree murder because, where defendant pointed a gun at someone in a vehicle and fired numerous rounds, it was logical to conclude that his actions were intentional and not merely reckless.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

 

211 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Harsch v. Miller


No. 100,149


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 280; 200 P.3d 467; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 40



February 13, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Harsch v. Miller, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 491 (Kan., Feb. 13, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Coffey district court, ERIC W. GODDERZ, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a judgment from the Coffey District Court (Kansas), which, after denying their motion to stay proceedings in their appeal from an appraisers' award in an eminent domain proceeding, dismissed the action for lack of prosecution when the property owners and their counsel failed to appear for trial. The district court held counsel in contempt and levied costs and fees against him.


OVERVIEW: The property owners sought to stay the proceedings in the district court pending the outcome of a constitutional challenge filed in federal court. After the district court denied their motion to stay proceedings, they filed a docketing statement with the clerk of the appellate courts. Although they acknowledged in their docketing statement that the order was not final, they did not perfect an interlocutory appeal under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-2102(c) (2007 Supp.). The court held that the district court did not lose jurisdiction to proceed with the trial when the property owners filed their docketing statement with the clerk of the appellate courts. The court observed that piecemeal appeals were undesirable and that the collateral order doctrine was a very narrow exception to the final decision requirement. The district court did not abuse its discretion in denying the motion to stay. The contempt order against counsel was void because the district court failed to comply with Kan. Stat. Ann. § 20-1203 when it did not include counsel's statement of the reasons for his absence in the journal entry; the lack of such a statement was a jurisdictional defect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's dismissal of the appeal from the appraisers' award. The court reversed the contempt order.

 

212 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bennett


No. 98,038


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 86; 200 P.3d 455; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 7



January 30, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Bennett, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 413 (Kan., Jan. 30, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in State v. Bennett, 39 Kan. App. 2d 890, 185 P.3d 320 (2008). Appeal from Dickinson district court; BENJAMIN J. SEXTON, judge.
State v. Bennett, 39 Kan. App. 2d 890, 185 P.3d 320, 2008 Kan. App. LEXIS 97 (2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of possession of methamphetamine and placed on probation. He appealed from one of the conditions of his probation, which required him to submit to random, suspicionless searches by community corrections or law enforcement officers. The Kansas Court of Appeals held that this condition was unconstitutional and unenforceable. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the State argued that the appellate court's decision would thwart efforts by law enforcement officers to rehabilitate probationers since those officers would be required to have reasonable suspicion to search. Because defendant was discharged while the case was pending before the supreme court, his counsel filed a notice of mootness. The supreme court held that although defendant was no longer subject to the conditions of probation in this case, the question as to the constitutionality of suspicionless searches was an issue that was capable of repetition, yet evading review. The supreme court further found that because parolees in Kansas had an expectation that they would not be subjected to suspicionless searches, it logically followed that because probationers had a greater expectation of privacy than parolees, searches of probationers in Kansas must also be based on a reasonable suspicion. Thus, the condition of defendant's probation subjecting him to random, nonconsensual, suspicionless searches violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § XV.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was affirmed, and the decision of the district court was reversed.

 

213 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Hodges v. Johnson


No. 97,062


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


288 Kan. 56; 199 P.3d 1251; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 11



January 30, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Hodges v. Johnson, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 406 (Kan., Jan. 30, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 39 Kan. App. 2d 220, 178 P.3d 59 (2008). Appeal from Saline district court; JEROME P. HELLMER, judge.
Hodges v. Johnson, 39 Kan. App. 2d 220, 178 P.3d 59, 2008 Kan. App. LEXIS 43 (2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions to the district court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A small claims court granted judgment against appellee used-car dealer for the cost of replacing a defective air conditioner in a vehicle purchased by appellant consumers. The used-car dealer appealed, and the district court affirmed but denied the consumers' request for attorney fees. Both parties appealed. The Kansas Court of Appeals reversed, and the consumers sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the consumers contended the appellate court erred in reversing the district court's decision. The supreme court agreed, finding that although the appellate court correctly acknowledged that the transaction involved in this case was subject to the statutory provisions of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 84-2-314, it erred when it attempted to determine the extent of the dealer's obligation to the consumers as a matter of law. The implied warranty of merchantability applied to the transaction as a matter of law under the statue, but the resolution of what the extent of the dealer's obligation was under the warranty was a factual determination. The attempt by the appellate court to convert this question into a legal determination amounted to error. Further, the circumstantial evidence was sufficient to establish that the defect in the air conditioner existed at the time of sale. And under the plain language of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 61-2709(a), the consumers were the successful on appeal and were entitled to reasonable attorney fees incurred on appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the district court with instructions.

 

214 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Prine


No. 93,345


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 713; 200 P.3d 1; 2009 Kan. LEXIS 1



January 16, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Prine, 2013 Kan. LEXIS 534 (Kan., May 31, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed December 1, 2006. Appeal from Reno district court; RICHARD J. ROME, judge.
State v. Prine, 146 P.3d 1113, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 1115 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming in part and dismissing in part is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of rape, aggravated criminal sodomy, and aggravated indecent liberties with a child because of his conduct with a six-year-old girl. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions, and defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the trial court erred in entering evidence about defendant's prior sexual abuse of two witnesses. The supreme court agreed, finding that although the fact that defendant molested other young girls in the past shed light on the existence of intent, absence of mistake or accident was not actually in issue. The State's introduction of evidence about defendant's hypothesis during his law enforcement interview that the victim could have become confused by certain other incidents of nonsexual touching was insufficient to support admission of contrary evidence by the State. Under these circumstances, the State could not open the door for itself to put the witnesses on the stand to rebut an innocent explanation advanced by defendant. The evidence it introduced from his interview bore no relationship to the defense theory of the case at trial. Under the signature standard, it was error to admit this evidence. As there was no physical evidence corroborating the victim's account, the State's entire case hinged entirely on the victim's credibility; thus, the admission of the evidence was not harmless.


OUTCOME: The convictions were reversed and the case was remanded for new trial.

 

215 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Conley


No. 99,279


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 696; 197 P.3d 837; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 741



December 19, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court, JOSEPH BRIBIESCA, judge.
State v. Conley, 270 Kan. 18, 11 P.3d 1147, 2000 Kan. LEXIS 810 (Oct. 27, 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a pro se motion to correct an allegedly illegal sentence. The Sedgwick District Court, Kansas, summarily denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of premeditated first-degree murder, and he was sentenced to a hard 40 life sentence. Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence on direct appeal. On appeal from the summary denial of his motion, defendant argued that the district court erred and/or abused its discretion in summarily denying the motion because: (1) res judicata was inapplicable to motions to correct illegal sentence; and (2) summary disposition was an inappropriate procedure for handling motions to correct illegal sentence. The appellate court concluded that res judicata applied to motions to correct illegal sentence filed pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3504. In this case, the question had been raised on direct appeal. Defendant next argued that summary denial was an inappropriate disposition of a motion filed pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3504(1); however, the appellate court concluded that the preliminary examination procedure was appropriate. The district court properly denied the motion summarily upon finding the motion to be barred on the basis of res judicata.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

216 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of A.A.T.


No. 98,740


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 590; 196 P.3d 1180; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 724



December 12, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Peterson v. Jackson, 556 U.S. 1184, 129 S. Ct. 2013, 173 L. Ed. 2d 1088, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 2974 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court; RICHARD T. BALLINGER, judge.
In re Adoption of A.A.T., 147 P.3d 1096, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 1173 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural father challenged a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) ruling that the father's attempt to set aside an adoption decree, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2213, and to assert a parental interest could not overcome the fully matured interests of the State and appellee adoptive parents in the permanency and stability of the adoption. The father also filed a motion for visitation.


OVERVIEW: The natural mother lied to the father about her pregnancy and the child's birth. She also lied to the district court regarding the father's identity. The father's parental rights were terminated at the adoption proceeding because he failed to appear and claim custodial rights, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2136(g). When the child was six months old, the father learned the truth about the baby, and he instituted proceedings to set aside the adoption. The district court held that the father's effort to set aside the adoption decree was time-barred, under the 30-day time limit of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2213. The court agreed that the decree could not be set aside based on newly discovered evidence, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-260(b)(2), because the father could have discovered the child with minimal diligence. The decree could also not be set aside on the ground of fraud, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-260(b)(3), because the mother did not qualify as an adverse party. The father's biological relationship with the child did not guarantee a liberty interest under the Due Process Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, absent an actual relationship of parental responsibility.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court. The father's motion for visitation was denied as moot.

 

217 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Thompson


No. 94,254


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 238; 200 P.3d 22; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 695



December 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified. January 28, 2009.
On remand at, Remanded by State v. Thompson, 2009 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 245 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed January 11, 2008. Appeal from McPherson district court; CARL B. ANDERSON, JR., judge.
State v. Thompson, 174 P.3d 458, 2008 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 23 (2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming in part, reversing in part, vacating the sentences in part, and remanding with directions to the district court on the issues subject to our grant of review is affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded to the district court with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State petitioned for review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kansas reversing defendant's conviction of possessing lithium metal with the intent to manufacture a controlled substance under K.S.A. 65-7006(a), and his sentences for manufacture of methamphetamine and possession of pseudoephedrine with the intent to manufacture a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that defendant's convictions of two violations of K.S.A. 65-7006(a) for possessing pseudoephedrine and lithium metal, both with the intent to manufacture a controlled substance, violated his double jeopardy rights and therefore the appellate court properly reversed defendant's conviction of possessing lithium metal, because: (1) defendant's actions of possessing lithium metal and pseudoephedrine with the intent to manufacture a controlled substance were part of one transaction of manufacturing methamphetamine and the record contained no evidence of an intervening event or fresh impulse; and (2) only a single unit of prosecution existed in K.S.A. 65-7006(a), as defendant's conduct was unitary. Defendant should have been sentenced to a severity level four drug felony for his conviction of possession of pseudoephedrine rather than a severity level one under Kansas' identical offense sentencing doctrine because K.S.A. 65-7006(a) and K.S.A. 65-4152(a)(3) contained overlapping, identical elements relating to the knowing possession of ephedrine or pseudoephedrine with the intent to manufacture.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing defendant's conviction of possessing lithium metal with the intent to manufacture a controlled substance was affirmed. Defendant's sentence for possession of pseudoephedrine with intent to manufacture was vacated and the case was remanded for resentencing to a severity level four drug felony. The judgment reversing defendant's sentence for manufacturing methamphetamine was reversed. The case was remanded with directions.

 

218 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Kan. Judicial Review v. Stout


No. 100,170


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 450; 196 P.3d 1162; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 706



December 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification of questions of law from the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit, Circuit Judge Carlos F. Lucero.
Kan. Judicial Review v. Stout, 519 F.3d 1107, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 7656 (10th Cir., 2008)



DISPOSITION: The answers to the certified questions are determined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the Kansas Judicial Review, a political action committee, a previous judicial candidate, and a state district court judge, filed an action against defendant members of the Kansas Commission on Judicial Qualifications (Commission) and the office of the Disciplinary Administrator, seeking injunctive and declaratory relief under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit certified questions of law.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contended that various canons of the Kansas Code of Judicial Conduct (Code), set forth in Kan. Sup. Ct. R. 601A, violated their rights to freedom of speech and freedom of assembly, under U.S. Const. amend. I, relating to whether judicial candidates could publicly announce their views on legal and political issues. The federal district court granted a preliminary injunction against enforcement of the judicial canons in question. On appeal, the court of appeals determined that plaintiffs' claims rested on sufficiently novel and determinative questions of state law that involved important state policy interests. The court had jurisdiction to answer certified questions, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-3201. The court recommended revisions to the Code. A judicial candidate might violate Kan. Sup. Ct. R. 601A, Canon 5A(3)(d)(i) and (ii), by answering a questionnaire asking for his or her views on disputed legal and political issues, depending on the questions asked. Additionally, a judicial candidate violated Kan. Sup. Ct. R. 601A, Canon 5C(2), by soliciting publicly stated support by personally collecting signatures for his or her nomination petition.


OUTCOME: The court determined the answers to the certified questions.

State v. Baker


No. 98,498


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 345; 197 P.3d 421; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 694



December 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Sharp, 289 Kan. 72, 210 P.3d 590, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 183 (2009)
Related proceeding at State v. Hollingsworth, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 1189 (Kan., Dec. 11, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Shawnee district court, JAMES M. MACNISH, JR., judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of felony murder and aiding and abetting a kidnapping in violation of K.S.A. 21-3420(c) and K.S.A. 21-3205(1). He received consecutive sentences of life without the possibility of parole for 20 years for the murder and 233 months in prison for the kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions and sentences for felony murder and aiding and abetting a kidnapping, but the supreme court affirmed, concluding that the trial court did not err in refusing defendant's request to instruct on compulsion; that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting three postmortem photographs of the victim's body; that the prosecutor did not commit reversible misconduct when using a sports team analogy to explain the concept of aiding and abetting to the jury; and that the trial court did not erroneously impose an enhanced sentence based upon prior criminal history without requiring those crimes be proved beyond a reasonable doubt. In regard to the compulsion instruction, there was at least one occasion of the many that the State argued where the alleged coercion or duress was noncontinuous and non-imminent. That overlapped with defendant's reasonable opportunity to escape or withdraw from the criminal activity. Nothing prevented defendant from leaving the camp when he was out of another homeless person's sight for at least 10 minutes.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

220 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Vasquez


Nos. 95,400, 95,401


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


287 Kan. 40; 194 P.3d 563; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 730



October 17, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Vasquez v. State, 331 P.3d 833, 2014 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 674 (Kan. Ct. App., Aug. 15, 2014)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion denied by Arroyo v. Pryor, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 142629 (D. Kan., Sept. 5, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Edwards district court; BRUCE T. GATTERMAN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated criminal cases, defendant appealed judgments from Edwards district court, Kansas, convicting him of three counts of first-degree murder; one count each of aggravated robbery, aggravated burglary, and felony theft; and two counts of misdemeanor theft. Defendant challenged Kansas' "hard 40" sentencing scheme and a sentence enhancement based on his commission of a heinous, atrocious, or cruel crime.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of executing his wife, her father, and her boyfriend. Coworkers testified that defendant and his wife were splitting up, that he had previously battered her, and that he threatened to kill her. The appeals court held that the district court erred, due a subsequent change in the law, by admitting evidence of marital discord independent of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455. However, applying § 60-455 retroactively, the appeals court held that the marital discord evidence was material and admissible to prove defendant's motive--rage and jealousy--for committing the triple murder. Defendant was not entitled to a voluntary manslaughter instruction because he was unable to show provocation, as his defense was that he did not enter the marital home on the day of the killings. The wife's hearsay statements to a police officer, to whom she gave defendant's guns several hours before she was killed, were properly admitted under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-460(d)(3). The evidence was insufficient to convict for aggravated burglary under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3716, as defendant still had legal access to the marital domicile. Defendant's enhanced capital murder sentence was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. Defendant's murder, aggravated robbery, felony theft, and misdemeanor theft convictions and their corresponding sentences were affirmed. His aggravated burglary conviction was reversed, and its corresponding sentence was vacated.

State v. Cook


No. 97,440


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 1098; 191 P.3d 294; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 462



September 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Saline district court; DANIEL L. HEBERT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Saline District Court (Kansas), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of premeditated first-degree murder, aggravated burglary, and criminal possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed the victim, who owed a drug debt, inside the victim's residence. Defendant failed to object at trial to asserted instructional error. The court found the evidence sufficient to establish premeditation under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(a), despite evidence that defendant might have intended to scare the victim. Defendant was not entitled to a self-defense instruction under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3211 because his conduct in committing a forcible felony precluded a self-defense claim pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3214(1) and because he provoked the victim and made no attempt to escape or to withdraw as provided in § 21-3214(3). He also was not entitled to a lesser included offense instruction for voluntary manslaughter under the alternative means of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3403(b). Although the district court instructed on aggravated burglary by alternative means, a unanimity instruction was not required because the State relied exclusively on one means. A failure to give a limiting instruction as to other crimes evidence did not affect the outcome. Grid box sentencing under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4704(e)(1) did not entail unconstitutional judicial factfinding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

 

222 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of G.L.V.


No. 97,546


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 1034; 190 P.3d 245; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 456



August 22, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 38 Kan. App. 2d 144, 163 P.3d 334 (2007). Appeal from Atchison district court; MARTIN J. ASHER, judge.
In re Adoption of G.L.V., 38 Kan. App. 2d 144, 163 P.3d 334, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 765 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court denied a stepparent adoption under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2136(d) (2007 Supp.), and the Court of Appeals of Kansas affirmed, holding that the natural father's consent was necessary because he had performed his parental duties during the two years preceding the adoption petition. The stepfather sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The stepfather argued that the district court and appellate court majority misinterpreted a 2006 amendment to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2136(d), which stated that the court could consider the best interests of a child and the fitness of a nonconsenting parent in determining whether a stepparent adoption should be granted. The court affirmed the interpretation of the lower courts. The plain language, both before and after the 2006 amendment, stated that the consent of a known father had to be given to a stepparent adoption unless the father failed or refused to assume the duties of a parent for two consecutive years preceding the petition. The best interests provision in the amendment did not permit a court to override the requirement of mandatory consent when a natural parent had assumed his or her parental responsibilities. Because the natural father had assumed a sufficient level of parental responsibility, his rights as a parent were entitled to constitutional protection under the Fourteenth Amendment. He had not consented to the adoption in this case, and his decision to withhold his consent could not be overridden absent compelling circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions of the district court and the appellate court.

State v. Warledo


No. 97,759


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 927; 190 P.3d 937; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 452



August 8, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court; BENJAMIN L. BURGESS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) convicting him of arson and premeditated first-degree murder. Defendant received a hard 50 life sentence following a finding of aggravating factors, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4636.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his mother by repeatedly stomping on her and then he set fire to her body. Defendant contended that the trial court erred by admitting into evidence statements he made to authorities before he was given the warnings required by Miranda. Defendant's spontaneous statements made to authorities in the interrogation room before he was verbally Mirandized were admissible because they were made before interrogation and before the Miranda safeguards were triggered. The court agreed that admission of evidence of defendant's prior crimes and civil wrongs violated Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455. However, the admission was not inconsistent with substantial justice given the overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt. Regarding the imposition of the hard 50 life sentence, defendant challenged the trial court's finding under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4636(f) that the murder was committed in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner. The court found no abuse of discretion in the sentencing court's decision that the aggravating circumstance outweighed defendant's mitigators. The court also held that the hard 50 sentencing scheme was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and hard 50 life sentence.

State v. Pollman


No. 93,947


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 881; 190 P.3d 234; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 450



August 8, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State v. Pollman, 2008 Kan. App. LEXIS 251 (Kan. Ct. App., Nov. 21, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed April 27, 2007. Appeal from McPherson district court; CARL B. ANDERSON, JR., judge.
State v. Pollman, 156 P.3d 690, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 446 (Kan. Ct. App., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court on the single issue subject to our grant of review is reversed and remanded to the Court of Appeals with directions. Judgment of the district court on this issue is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the results of breath alcohol concentration (BAC) and preliminary breath tests (PBT), defendant was convicted of operating a motor vehicle while his BAC was.08 or more in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567(a)(2), (f). The Court of Appeals of Kansas reversed the order denying the motion. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that defendant was seized under the Fourth Amendment and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15 because, given that the officer retained defendant's driver's license, a second officer told the first officer that defendant needed to be checked, and the officer asked a potentially incriminating question about the consumption of alcohol, a reasonable person would not have felt free to refuse the officer's request or otherwise terminate the encounter. The court held that the detention was permissible under the Fourth Amendment and Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-2402 because the officer had a reasonable suspicion that defendant had committed a crime, as: (1) defendant had refused to follow lawful requests to leave the immediate area of his wife's traffic stop; (2) defendant admitted that he had consumed a few beers; and (3) the officer smelled the odor of alcohol on defendant's breath, which occurred after the officer had observed defendant driving his motorcycle. Only after these factors created a reasonable suspicion did the officer perform the field sobriety tests and PBT.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing the denial of defendant's motion to suppress was reversed and the case was remanded to the appellate court for consideration of defendant's other issues.

State v. Gallegos


No. 98,949


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 869; 190 P.3d 226; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 447



August 1, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Rice district court; MIKE KEELEY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Rice District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of premeditated, first-degree murder and criminal possession of a firearm. The court imposed sentences of life imprisonment with no possibility of parole for 25 years for the murder conviction and 9 months' imprisonment for the firearm offense, to be served consecutively. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended, inter alia, that the district court erred by not instructing the jury on voluntary manslaughter as a lesser included offense of first-degree murder, and by providing an instruction that allegedly shifted the burden of proof to defendant to prove his innocence. The supreme court disagreed, finding first that the evidence of an argument between defendant and the victim before the victim's death failed to demonstrate any legally sufficient provocation that would have warranted an instruction on voluntary manslaughter. When the victim told defendant to calm down, defendant responded by shooting him five times, including three times in the head. As such, the district court did not err when it instructed the jury on only second-degree murder as a lesser included offense of premeditated, first-degree murder as the evidence did not justify a conviction for voluntary manslaughter. Even if the instruction was in some way erroneous, any possible error was invited by defendant, who proposed the exact language in question before trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Johnson


No. 96,681


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 824; 190 P.3d 207; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 445



August 1, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Johnson v. State, 2014 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 794 (Kan. Ct. App., Oct. 10, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Douglas district court; MICHAEL J. MALONE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, dismissed in part, vacated in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his consecutive sentences imposed by the Douglas District Court (Kansas) following his four convictions of attempted second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The grid block for each of defendant's convictions indicated a prison sentence of 55, 59, or 61 months, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4704. Defendant contended that the consecutive sentences for the longest prison term stated in a Kansas sentencing guidelines presumptive grid block were unconstitutional. He argued that the factors justifying the longer term were not proved beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4704(e)(1) did not require judicial fact-finding and granted discretion to the sentencing judge to impose any of the prison terms stated in the grid block. The crimes involved a random shooting at an apartment complex that could have resulted in several deaths. The court also held that defendant's sentences were statutorily and constitutionally permissibly presumptive and could not be appealed, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4721(c)(1). Defendant also contended that the sentencing judge erred by ordering him to reimburse the State Board of Indigents' Defense Services (BID) for attorney fees without first considering his ability to pay. The court agreed that the judge was required to make findings pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4513.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court vacated the part of the appeal challenging the presumptive sentences because the court lacked jurisdiction. The court remanded for further findings on defendant's ability to pay the BID fees.

 

227 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cook


No. 98,671


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 766; 187 P.3d 1283; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 393



July 25, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Wyandotte district court; MICHAEL GROSKO, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment of the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas), which dismissed a charge of failure to register as a sex offender under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4904 (Supp. 2006) against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Upon his release, defendant completed a sex offender registration form. He filed no additional registration forms. Nearly four months later, he was arrested for a domestic battery. The following month, defendant was sent a registered letter at the address provided on the registration form. It was returned marked return to sender. Six months later, the Kansas Legislature changed violation of the registration statute from a severity-level 10 nonperson felony to a severity level 5 person felony under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4903 (Supp. 2006). Nearly five months after the change defendant was charged with violating § 22-4904, as a severity level 5 person felony. Upon review, the court held that because the actual crime for which defendant was being sentenced was committed after the effective date of the amended statute, there was no ex post facto violation under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1 and § 9, cl. 3. Defendant had notice that each failure to register over a 30-day period constituted a new crime and was already aware under the pre-amendment version that he had a duty to register. Accordingly, defendant could be charged under § 22-4903.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's judgment and remanded to the district court with directions to reinstate the charge of failure to register as a sex offender pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4903 (Supp. 2006).

 

228 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Hall v. Dillon Cos.


No. 99,476


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 777; 189 P.3d 508; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 395



July 25, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Workers Compensation Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged the percentage of disability determined by the Kansas Workers Compensation Board. An administrative law judge (ALJ) found that the employee suffered from a 10 percent permanent partial disability in her left upper extremity and a 10 percent permanent partial disability in her right upper extremity. The Board had affirmed the ALJ's decision.


OVERVIEW: The employee argued she was entitled to an additional 6 percent impairment for instability in the wrist of her right hand. A doctor testified by deposition that the employee had an instability in the right wrist that was additional to a carpal tunnel injury noted by other experts. The court found no basis for modifying the disability rating. Neither the ALJ nor the Board had disregarded the doctor's diagnosis, and the employee made no showing that bias, passion, or prejudice played a role in the decisions. At least five medical consultants, including the doctor, had examined the employee as a consequence of the pain she reported. Four of them made no mention of wrist instability. The plain language of the workers compensation statutes created no parallel-injury rule and established a rebuttable presumption that a claimant was incapable of engaging in substantial and gainful employment. The record did not support the employee's assertion that the parallel injuries to her arms made her work more challenging or limited her tasks more than an injury to either arm alone would have done. The employee did not show she had suffered a loss of due process by not receiving fair compensation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the Board.

State v. Reid


No. 93,646


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 494; 186 P.3d 713; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 332



June 27, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Johnson district court; JOHN ANDERSON III, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury before the Johnson District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of first-degree premeditated murder of a store's assistant manager and aggravated robbery of the business. Defendant received a hard 50 sentence. Defendant appealed from the convictions and sentence.


OVERVIEW: A witness, who was riding his bicycle toward a convenience store, heard gunfire and smelled gun powder. The store's assistant manager died, and money was found to be missing from the store. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence of his termination from the store for stealing was not relevant to prove motive. Nevertheless, when viewed through the knowledge factor of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455, the evidence in the instant case revealed that the crimes were most likely committed by someone with intimate knowledge of the store operations. Moreover, the evidence was not more prejudicial than it was probative because defendant was acquitted of other honesty-based crimes and a theft. Defendant also argued that his trial should have been severed from that of his co-defendant's because of antagonistic defenses. The co-defendant's testimony, however, did not establish that defendant and the co-defendant had antagonistic defenses. Both denied involvement in the robbery and murder, and neither implicated the other. Finally, the trial court did not violate defendant's due process and jury trial rights by failing to instruct the jury on lesser included homicides and robbery.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence.

 

230 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re L.M.


No. 96,197


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 460; 186 P.3d 164; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 328



June 20, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed December 22, 2006. Appeal from Finney district court; PHILIP C. VIEUX, judge.
In re L.M., 147 P.3d 1096, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 1170 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Kansas) affirming appellant's juvenile adjudication for aggravated sexual battery, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-727 (Supp. 2005), and being a minor in possession of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: The facts leading up to the charges allegedly involved a sexually suggestive confrontation between appellant and a neighbor. Appellant's request for a jury trial was denied. Appellant claimed that he should have received a jury trial and he argued that sweeping changes to juvenile justice procedures in Kansas merited renewed scrutiny under applicable constitutional protections. Appellant's first argument was made pursuant to the Sixth Amendment. The supreme court agreed with appellant because the proceedings under the Revised Kansas Juvenile Justice Code (KJJC), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 38-2301 et seq. (Supp. 2006), were now more akin to criminal proceedings. Appellant also asserted that he had a right to a jury trial under Kan. Const. Bill Rights §§ 1, 5, and 10. The supreme court noted that the KJJC repeatedly referred to its proceedings as a prosecution. The proceedings under the KJJC fit within the meaning of the phrase "all prosecutions" as set forth in § 10, and juveniles had a right to a jury trial under the Kansas Constitution. Finally, the supreme court noted that the right to a jury trial was new rule of procedure that did not operate retroactively.


OUTCOME: The matter was reversed and remanded for a new trial before a jury.

 

231 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Smith


No. 96,189


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 402; 184 P.3d 890; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 204



May 30, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Kan. v. Smith, 555 U.S. 1062, 129 S. Ct. 628, 172 L. Ed. 2d 639, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 8840 (U.S., Dec. 1, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed January 26, 2007. Appeal from Cowley district court; NICHOLAS M. ST. PETER, judge.
State v. Smith, 150 P.3d 335, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 68 (Kan. Ct. App., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with possession of methamphetamine and misdemeanor possession of drug paraphernalia. The trial court granted her motion to suppress evidence seized during a search of her purse and person and to suppress incriminating statements. The Court of Appeals (Kansas) reversed, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a car that was stopped for traffic violations. The State conceded that there was no basis for a search of her purse other than consent. The court of appeals found that an officer could question her about matters unrelated to the purpose of the stop and that her responding consent was therefore valid. In reversing, the court held first that defendant was seized for purposes of the Fourth Amendment and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15 after an officer pulled behind the car, blocked it, and activated emergency lights. The court also held, contrary to the court of appeals ruling, that U.S. Supreme Court case law in the context of a warranted home search did not allow law enforcement officers to expand the scope of a traffic stop to include a search not related to the purpose of the stop, even if a detainee gave permission for the search. Rather, the court continued to adhere to the long-standing rule that consensual searches during the period of a detention for a traffic stop were invalid under the Fourth Amendment and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15. Therefore, evidence from the search of defendant's purse was properly suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals reversing the district court. The court affirmed the judgment of the district court and remanded the case.

 

232 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Greever


No. 95,303


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 124; 183 P.3d 788; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 180



May 16, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended, June 5, 2009.
On remand at State v. Greever, 197 P.3d 904, 2008 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 983 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 37 Kan. App. 2d 145, 150 P.3d 918 (2007). Appeal from Reno district court; TIMOTHY J. CHAMBERS, judge.
State v. Greever, 37 Kan. App. 2d 145, 150 P.3d 918, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 89 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed, and the case is remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Reno County District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of possession of marijuana and possession of marijuana without drug tax stamps affixed. The Kansas Court of Appeals decided that the evidence in question should have been suppressed because the initial traffic stop was illegal, and reversed defendant's convictions. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that there was an illegal seizure of his person under the Fourth Amendment because the initial traffic stop was not supported by reasonable suspicion that defendant had committed a traffic violation. The supreme court found that the evidence supported a conclusion that the encounter between defendant and police officer was not voluntary. It was clear from the evidence that defendant failed to give the warning proscribed by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1548. Defendant did not signal 100 feet before making his turn. The officer testified that he believed that defendant was committing a traffic offense, and under Kansas law, the officer was authorized to stop defendant for such an offense even though that stop may have been pretextual. The officer observed defendant violated Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1548 and as such, he had probable cause to stop defendant for that violation. Defendant's two additional arguments were not addressed by the court of appeals and were not included in the State's petition for review. Thus, the court of appeals had to consider the remaining issues on remand.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the district court's denial of the motion to suppress was affirmed; the case was remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the two remaining issues raised and not resolved in defendant's original appeal.

 

233 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Scott


No. 83,801


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 54; 183 P.3d 801; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 182



May 16, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court, DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.
State v. Wakefield, 267 Kan. 116, 977 P.2d 941, 1999 Kan. LEXIS 231 (1999)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, sentence of death vacated, and case remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) convicted defendant of capital murder, premeditated first-degree murder, aggravated burglary, criminal possession of a firearm, and felony theft. He was sentenced to death for capital murder, with consecutive sentences of life imprisonment for premeditated first-degree murder, 51 months for aggravated burglary, 9 months for criminal possession of a firearm, and 7 months for felony theft. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction for the premeditated first-degree murder of one victim under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(a) was necessarily proved once the capital murder of both victims under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3439(a)(6) was proved. Thus, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3107(2)(d), defendant's convictions were multiplicitous in the absence of clear and unambiguous legislative intent authorizing multiple prosecutions for the same conduct. So, when a plain reading of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3439(a)(6) and its legislative history did not reveal the legislature's intent to override the acknowledged reach of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3107(2)(d), the supreme court set aside defendant's first-degree premeditated murder conviction because it was multiplicitous with his capital murder conviction and, therefore, in violation of the Fifth Amendment's Double Jeopardy Clause and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 10. The supreme court also set aside defendant's death sentence and remanded his case to the trial court for a new sentencing hearing, because the trial court's instructions to the jury did not inform the jurors that they did not have to unanimously agree regarding the existence of mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed, except for the crime of premeditated murder, which was reversed. The imposition of the death penalty was set aside, and the case was remanded to the trial court for another sentencing proceeding.

 

234 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Tiller v. Corrigan


Nos. 99,951; 99,972; 100,042


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


286 Kan. 30; 182 P.3d 719; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 92



May 6, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Original actions in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Writs of mandamus granted in part and cases remanded to district court with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three consolidated original actions in mandamus against respondent judges were prompted by subpoenas duces tecum issued by a Sedgwick County, Kansas, grand jury summoned in response to a citizen petition under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3301, which called for investigation of alleged illegal abortions and other violations by petitioner doctor and others performing professional services at petitioner health care company.


OVERVIEW: The doctor and health care company challenged the constitutionality of the citizen-petition grand jury statutes, and petitioner Kansas Attorney General asserted that a grand jury had no authority to issue a subpoena duces tecum. The supreme court found that the judiciary was to assume the oversight of the grand jury once a petition was filed. Any claim that the process was being abused did not follow from an unconstitutional legislative invasion of the judicial or executive function. Under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3008(1), grand juries possessed the authority to issue subpoenas duces tecum. The district court had to assess whether each of the subpoenas were relevant. It had to assess the degree of need the grand jury had in obtaining the material described in each subpoena and to balance that need against the burden imposed upon the doctor and health care company. The district court had to make sure that the grand jury had not engaged in an arbitrary fishing expedition and that the targets were not selected and subpoenas issued out of malice or with intent to harass. The district court had to consider the competing interests of the State and the patients and apply a balancing test.


OUTCOME: The petitions for writs mandamus were granted in part and the matter was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

Denning v. Kan. Pub. Emples. Ret. Sys.


No. 98,111


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 1045; 180 P.3d 564; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 82



March 28, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Shawnee district court; DAVID E. BRUNS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of an order from the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which upheld an order from defendant, the Kansas Public Employees Retirement System (KPERS), suspending his retirement payments.


OVERVIEW: In 1978, the employee worked for a county sheriff's department and participated in the Kansas Police and Firemen's Retirement System (KP&F). Between 1996 and 2002, he worked as the chief of police for a city. In 2001, the city switched from KPERS to KP&F, and his participation switched as well. When he retired in 2003, he began to draw his KP&F retirement benefits. He was later elected county sheriff. Because of the annual earnings cap in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 74-4957(5) (Supp. 2007), administrators of KPERS, which oversaw KP&F, suspended his retirement payments. The KPERS Board and the trial court upheld the decision. On appeal, the court found that the trial court did not err in interpreting § 74-4957(5). The statute was not void for vagueness. Because the court could not conclude that the word "during" or the statutory subsection in which it appeared was ambiguous, it certainly could not conclude that either was constitutionally vague. The statute did not violate the Contracts Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, because the 1994 replacement of a time limitation with an earnings limitation constituted an exchange with offsetting benefits and detriments for workers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

236 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Morrison v. Sebelius


No. 98,691


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 875; 179 P.3d 366; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 70



March 11, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Original action in quo warranto.



DISPOSITION: Judgment for the petitioner is granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State, through the Attorney General, brought an original action in quo warranto against respondent Governor, challenging the constitutionality of the judicial trigger provision, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4015(i), of the Kansas Funeral Privacy Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4015 et seq., on the grounds that the legislature violated the separation of powers doctrine. The Attorney General also requested an order severing the provision.


OVERVIEW: The judicial trigger provision, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4015(i), sought an unconstitutional remedy that violated the separation of powers doctrine because a lawsuit under the provision did not present an actual case or controversy, but sought an advisory opinion. The provision also purported to make the court an advisor to the legislature, thereby usurping the legislature's duty to make a preliminary judgment on the constitutionality of inoperative legislative provisions. Thus, the legislature violated the separation of powers doctrine by directing the Attorney General to file an action challenging the constitutionality of the funeral protest provisions of the Kansas Funeral Privacy Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4015 et seq. The judicial trigger provision could not be severed from the Act as severance would have broadened the effect of the Act in a manner contrary to the express directions of the legislature. The legislature directed that the provisions became operative if and when determined constitutional. That determination had not been made, and it violated the separation of powers doctrine to make the provisions operative in a manner contrary to the legislature' explicit directions.


OUTCOME: The Attorney General's petition was granted, but the request for an order severing the judicial trigger provision was denied.

 

237 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Holt


No. 96,744


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 760; 175 P.3d 239; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 14



February 1, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Wabaunsee district court, GARY L. NAFSINGER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Wabaunsee District Court (Kansas) of first-degree premeditated murder and conspiracy to commit murder. Defendant was sentenced to life in prison without the possibility of parole for 25 years for the murder and 131 months' imprisonment for the conspiracy. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that because there was insufficient evidence to support an aiding and abetting instruction, the trial court erred when it overruled his objection to giving it. The supreme court disagreed. In examining the evidence, defendant pointed to the State's evidence that he was the shooter, suggesting that based on that evidence, the jury could not have concluded that the accomplice was the shooter and defendant therefore the aider and abettor. As the State noted, however, while its prosecution theory was that defendant was the shooter, the entire defense was that the accomplice was the shooter. The defense itself elicited testimony indicating that the accomplice confessed to another inmate about being the shooter. There was also testimony from which the jury could have concluded that defendant aided and abetted the accomplice as the shooter because defendant enlisted the accomplice to help with the crime. The aiding and abetting instruction was proper because the jury reasonably could have concluded from the totality of the evidence that defendant was an aider or abettor to the accomplice, even though the State's theory was that defendant was the principal.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the district court for a redetermination of the defense attorney reimbursement fee. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.
