Martin v. Kan. Dep't of Revenue


No. 94,033


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 625; 176 P.3d 938; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 15



February 1, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 36 Kan. App. 2d 561, 142 P.3d 735 (2006). Appeal from Johnson district court; KEVIN P. MORIARTY, judge.
Martin v. Kan. Dep't of Revenue, 36 Kan. App. 2d 561, 142 P.3d 735, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 920 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Kansas Department of Revenue suspended appellee driver's driver's license when he was pulled over by an officer due to a malfunctioning rear brake light and subsequently failed field sobriety tests and a chemical breath test. A district court reversed the suspension upon finding the officer lacked reasonable suspicion to initiate the traffic stop. The Kansas Court of Appeals reversed, and the driver sought review.


OVERVIEW: The questions on review were, inter alia, whether the Department was permitted to decide the merits of the driver's constitutional challenge in the administrative hearing on license suspension; and whether a meritorious constitutional challenge to the underlying traffic stop required the district court to apply the exclusionary rule and reverse the license suspension. In view of the fact that a driver's license was a privilege rather than a right, the supreme court held that the exclusion of U.S. Const. amend. IV and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15 issues from a Department decision in administrative suspension hearings under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1020(h)(2)(A)-(H) did not violate procedural due process. The supreme court agreed with the driver that the arresting officer misunderstood and misapplied the ordinance regarding how many rear brake lights on a vehicle had to be functioning and thus lacked constitutional authority to stop the driver; however, the balance between the benefits and costs of application of the exclusionary rule, when employed in the driver's license suspension proceedings, tipped in favor of the Department and against the driver.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court was affirmed. The judgment of the district court reversing the suspension was reversed.

Tolen v. State


No. 95,106


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 672; 176 P.3d 170; 2008 Kan. LEXIS 10



February 1, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Saline district court; DAN D. BOYER, judge.
State v. Tolen, 40 P.3d 341, 2002 Kan. App. LEXIS 18 (Kan. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Saline District Court, Kansas, which dismissed his motion under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-1507 for failing to file it within the one-year statutory limit of § 60-1507(f).


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who had been convicted of rape and aggravated criminal sodomy in February 1999, argued that § 60-1507(f) was unconstitutional because it did not include language granting a grace period for preexisting claims that were final before the one-year time limitation became effective. The instant court concluded that the district court properly denied appellant's § 60-1507 motion as untimely. Appellant lacked standing to challenge the statute's constitutionality. Appellant's direct appeal outcome became final on March 20, 2002. Section 60-1507(f) did not take effect until July 1, 2003. Even if a one-year grace period was permitted, it expired on July 1, 2004, and appellant did not file his § 60-1507 motion until January 18, 2005. Appellant was attempting to argue a constitutional infirmity that could not help him even if it merited a cure.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State ex rel. Slusher v. City of Leavenworth


No. 96,414


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 438; 172 P.3d 1154; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 829; 183 L.R.R.M. 2493



December 14, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Leavenworth district court; DAVID J. KING, judge.
State ex rel. Slusher v. City of Leavenworth, 279 Kan. 789, 112 P.3d 131, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 337 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Appeal affirmed and cross-appeal denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police department employees filed a petition for writ of mandamus in the Leavenworth District Court (Kansas), alleging that each was denied promotion by appellee city in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 73-201, a veterans' preference statute. The district court dismissed their petition, but the supreme court reversed and remanded. On remand, the district court granted the employees summary judgment. All parties sought review.


OVERVIEW: The employees complained that, although the district court granted them summary judgment as to the applicability of the veteran's preference, it should have ordered the city to promote them without further proceedings. The city, in turn, claimed that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 73-201 was unconstitutional and that the employees had not exhausted their administrative remedies. The supreme court held that: (1) the district court's order for the city to reopen the hiring process and apply a meaningful veteran's preference was the correct remedy because the city retained discretion in applying the veteran's preference; (2) the district court's refusal to declare the employees "competent" within the meaning of § 73-201 was proper because such a ruling would have been neither legally nor factually supportable; (3) § 73-201 did not violate the Privileges and Immunities Clause of Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 2 or the due process and equal protection guarantees of both state and federal Constitutions because the statute vested discretion in the hiring authority; and (4) given the relief sought, the employees' failure to exhaust administrative remedies was not fatal to their mandamus petition.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

242 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In the Interest of K.M.H.


No. 96,102


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 53; 169 P.3d 1025; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 644



October 26, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hendrix v. Harrington, 555 U.S. 937, 129 S. Ct. 36, 172 L. Ed. 2d 239, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 6341 (U.S., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Shawnee district court; ROBERT J. SCHMISSEUR, assigned judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee mother filed a child in need of care petition in the Shawnee District Court (Kansas) to establish that appellant sperm donor had no parental rights under Kansas law. The donor sued for determination of his paternity. The district court sustained the mother's motion to dismiss, ruling that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 38-1114(f) was controlling and constitutional. The donor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The donor argued, inter alia, that the requirement of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 38-1114(f) that any opt-out agreement between an unmarried mother and a known sperm donor be "in writing" resulted in an equal protection violation. The supreme court disagreed. Section 38-1114(f) envisioned that both married and unmarried women could become parents without engaging in sexual intercourse, either because of personal choice or because a husband or partner was infertile, impotent, or ill. It encouraged men who were able and willing to donate sperm to such women by protecting the men from later unwanted claims for support from the mothers or the children. It protected women recipients as well, preventing potential claims of donors to parental rights and responsibilities, in the absence of an agreement. The requirement that any such agreement be in writing enhanced predictability, clarity, and enforceability. If the parties desired an arrangement different from the statutory norm, they were free to provide for it, as long as they did so in writing. Therefore, the application of § 38-1114(f) to the donor did not violate equal protection.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Garcia


No. 93,964


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


285 Kan. 1; 169 P.3d 1069; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 641



October 26, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Kan. v. Garcia, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 99 (Kan., May 22, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Sedgwick district court; REBECCA L. PILSHAW, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) of rape and first-degree felony murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Application of the amendment to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3106 to revive defendant's previously time-barred prosecution for rape violated the Ex Post Facto Clause. Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his felony murder conviction. The supreme court disagreed. The felony of attempted rape also could serve as the basis for the felony murder, and an attempt did not require penetration. Such evidence was ample. The victim was found blindfolded, lying face up on the floor of her apartment. Her legs were spread a couple of feet apart, and her dress was pulled up to her armpits. Evidence also revealed her wrists had been bound, and her panty hose had been wadded up and cast aside. The coroner essentially opined that she had been sexually assaulted. Testimony established that two partially smoked cigarettes containing only defendant's DNA were found in an ashtray in the apartment near the victim's body. Defendant also admitted he had been in the victim's apartment complex around the time of the murder. Shortly after her body had been found, defendant asked witnesses if they had heard about the victim, calling her by her first name.


OUTCOME: The felony-murder conviction was affirmed. The rape conviction was reversed and the case was remanded with directions to vacate the rape sentence.

In re Adoption of X.J.A.


No. 96,003


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 853; 166 P.3d 396; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 486



September 7, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 36 Kan. App. 2d 621, 142 P.3d 327 (2006). Appeal from Ford district court; VAN Z. HAMPTON, judge.
In re Adoption of X.J.A., 36 Kan. App. 2d 621, 142 P.3d 327, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 919 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed in part and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals of Kansas reversed without remand a district court's finding that an adoption consent substantially complied with the adoption statutes and decision to terminate a mother's parental rights and grant parental rights to adoptive parents. The appeals court held that the consent did not comply with the Uniform Law on Notarial Acts, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 53-501 et. seq. Further review followed.


OVERVIEW: The natural mother signed a consent to adoption. The form was taken to a notary who completed the acknowledgement outside the mother's presence. The mother also delivered the newborn to the adoptive parents. Before the adoption was final, mother moved to withdraw her consent, prompting a 2-day hearing. The district court applied the doctrine of substantial compliance to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2114, denied the mother's motion, and granted the adoption. Most of the district court's finding was supported by substantial competent evidence, e.g., regarding the mother's signing the consent and her understanding of what she was signing. The district court correctly found that the adoptive parents met their burden on the issue of the mother's signature and that the mother had the burden to rebut that evidence, which she failed to do. However, the district court erred in shifting the burden of proving involuntary consent to the mother after her signature was established. A remand was needed to decide if the consent was voluntary. Substantial evidence supported the finding that the adoptive parents were conmon-law husband and wife, and the district court had jurisdiction to grant the adoption.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals. The court affirmed the judgment of the district court, in part, finding that the district court correctly applied the substantial compliance doctrine to find the mother signed the consent, but the court remanded the matter to the district court to determine if the mother's consent was voluntary.

Dillon Real Estate Co. v. City of Topeka


No. 95,162 1


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 662; 163 P.3d 298; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 474



July 27, 2007, Opinion Filed


NOTICE:


1 1 REPORTER'S NOTES: Justice Donald L. Allegrucci heard oral arguments and participated in the final decision of this case prior to his retirement.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Shawnee district court; CHARLES E. ANDREWS, JR., judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an owner, an improvement district, and a township, sought review of a decision of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which granted appellee City of Topeka's motion for summary judgment and allowed it to annex part of the improvement district, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-520.


OVERVIEW: The district, township, and owner of part of the annexed property contested the City's efforts to unilaterally annex the district. The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to the City and remanded the case with directions to grant summary judgment to the owner, district, and township. The court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-520(c) barred the City's unilateral annexation of part of the district; therefore, because the City failed to proceed in a legislatively authorized way, via a county approval annexation under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-521, its annexation was a nullity. The owner was not estopped by its predecessors' consents from asserting Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-520(c). The court held that the district court did not abuse its discretion in permitting the state to intervene. The court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-520(c) had a rational basis and did not violate Kan. Const. art. 12, § 5(a) but operated uniformly to all cities in the vicinity of all 33 improvement districts. The court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-536 did not limit the application of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-520(c) to annexation cases without landowner consent and, thus, did not bar an action contesting annexation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded the case with directions to vacate the judgment for the City and to enter judgment for the owner, district, and township.

 

247 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Noah


No. 91,353


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 608; 162 P.3d 799; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 479



July 27, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed July 14, 2006. Appeal from Rooks district court; EDWARD E. BOUKER, judge.
State v. Noah, 137 P.3d 1093, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 631 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is reversed and remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of aggravated indecent liberties. The Kansas Court of Appeals reversed the convictions, concluding that the trial court improperly admitted hearsay statements from the child victim in violation of defendant's U.S. Const. amend. VI right to confrontation. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the State argued that the child victim's statements were admissible because defendant had a prior opportunity to cross-examine the victim during the preliminary hearing. The supreme court disagreed, finding first that Crawford's constitutional principles applied to defendant's direct appeal because it was pending when Crawford was decided. Further, the limitation of defendant's cross-examination caused by the victim's inability to continue when she became upset and started crying due to the questioning prohibited defendant from engaging in otherwise appropriate cross-examination aimed at exposing facts from which the jury could have drawn inferences about the victim's reliability. Because the victim's statements were critical to the prosecution's case in that there were no eyewitnesses or physical evidence to establish the abuse alleged by the victim, and because any evidence of the victim's motive may have had a substantial impact on the jury because the case turned on whether the jury believed the victim's accusations or defendant's denial, the erroneous admission of the victim's statements in violation of defendant's right to confrontation was not harmless.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment reversing the trial court's judgment was affirmed. The trial court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

 

248 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Voyles


No. 92,030


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 239; 160 P.3d 794; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 362



June 22, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 34 Kan. App. 2d 110, 116 P.3d 720 (2005). Appeal from Kingman district court; LARRY T. SOLOMON, judge.
State v. Voyles, 34 Kan. App. 2d 110, 116 P.3d 720, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 693 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kansas affirming his convictions of two counts per victim for aggravated indecent solicitation of a child in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3511 and two counts per victim of aggravated criminal sodomy in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3506(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for forcing the two victims, his daughter and stepdaughter, to perform oral sex on him on a number of occasions. On appeal, the court held that the district court's failure to provide a unanimity instruction requiring unanimous agreement on a specific act for a particular count was reversible error because it was a multiple acts case, as defendant's conduct involved different times and different locations, demonstrating acts that were separate and distinct from each other. Accepting a witness' testimony that the victims told her of incidents at five different locations, potentially 20 different acts or offenses were committed, but defendant was charged with only eight. There were some discrepancies between the victims, some discrepancies between the victims and the witness, and some inconsistencies in one victim's statements. There was a real possibility that the jury would have returned a different verdict if the unanimity instruction had been given. The court further held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3506(a)(1) was constitutional because it implicated the legitimate goal of protecting the wellbeing of children from adult sexual predators.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were reversed and the case was remanded.

 

249 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Steffes v. City of Lawrence


No. 96,838


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 380; 160 P.3d 843; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 367



June 22, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Douglas district court; JACK A. MURPHY judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bar owner petitioned for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief prohibiting appellee city from enforcing Ordinance No. 7782, City of Lawrence, Kan., City Code §§ 9-801 to 9-814, which regulated smoking in public places. The Douglas County District Court (Kansas) denied the bar owner's motion for a temporary injunction, denied his motion to reconsider, and denied his petition. The bar owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Ordinance No. 7782 prohibited smoking in public places and places of employment and imposed certain obligations upon the owner, manager, or other person having control of such building or other areas where smoking was prohibited. The bar owner argued that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and preempted by state law. Under the Home Rule Amendment to the Kansas Constitution, Kan. Const. art. 12, § 5, the appellate court determined that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4010 did not preempt the ordinance because there was no conflict between state and local law since (1) under Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-4010 and 21-4013, the legislature invited cities to regulate smoking in public places to the maximum extent possible, and (2) both the state and local laws were prohibitory. The ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague, because its "has knowledge" requirement adequately stated the requisite intent and it sufficiently specified the type of conduct prohibited and the requisite level of enforcement by a club owner. Finally, the district court properly denied temporary and permanent injunctive relief because the bar owner's arguments were properly rejected on the merits.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed.

 

250 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gaudina


No. 95,854


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 354; 160 P.3d 854; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 369



June 22, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief dismissed at Gaudina v. State, 2007 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 561 (Kan. Ct. App., July 20, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed October 20, 2006. Appeal from Johnson district court; STEPHEN R. TATUM, judge.
State v. Gaudina, 144 P.3d 81, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 1045 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kansas which affirmed a district court's refusal to reduce defendant's term of postconviction release with time he served prison in excess of the term of imprisonment that was imposed at resentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was originally sentenced to 150 months in prison with 36 months of post-release supervision after he was convicted of aggravated burglary and aggravated battery. After Apprendi was decided, defendant was resentenced to 77 months in prison and 36 months of post-release supervision. Defendant argued that because he had already served 32 months in prison beyond his 77-month sentence, he should receive credit toward his post-release supervision. On appeal, the court disagreed. The court held that post-release supervision was a separate segment of the sentence and any credit for time spent in confinement was credited against time spent incarcerated, not against post-release supervision. Defendant cited no statute or regulation allowing penal credit to be applied to the period of post-release supervision. The trial court's ruling did not violate the prohibition against double jeopardy under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 10 because a subsequent change in the law occurred between defendant's sentencing and resentencing that decreased his term of imprisonment and he was not subjected to an increased term of imprisonment or post-release supervision.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

251 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Young Partners, LLC v. Bd. of Educ.


No. 97,087


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 397; 160 P.3d 830; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 368



June 22, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Grant district court, TOM R. SMITH, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged a decision from the Grant District Court (Kansas), which enjoined it from bringing an eminent domain action against appellee owner's reversionary interest in certain property owned by the district.


OVERVIEW: The school district acquired the property in 1947, but it was subject to a reversionary interest. The school district used and maintained the property for over 50 years. During those years, the school district constructed substantial improvements on the property. Later, it sought to bring an eminent domain action to acquire the reversionary interest under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-8212a(b). The owner contended that this was unconstitutional, and the district court agreed. This appeal followed. In reversing, the supreme court held that the Kansas Legislature deemed that it was in the public interest for the school district to protect its public investment against a reversionary interest by authorizing condemnation of the reversionary interest. The requirement that a taking be made for a "public purpose" under the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments was fulfilled by the two conditions set forth in § 72-8212a(b). The provisions of § 72-8212a were not unconstitutional, and a public purpose existed for the condemnation action filed by the school district. Finally, the Contract Clause was not violated because the power of eminent domain existed prior to the execution of the contract.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded.

 

252 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Stano


No. 95,138


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 126; 159 P.3d 931; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 329



June 8, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Stano v. State, 247 P.3d 235, 2011 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 177 (Kan. Ct. App., Mar. 4, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Shawnee district court, MATTHEW J. DOWD, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Shawnee District Court (Kansas) found defendant guilty of first-degree, premeditated murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim was shot in the head and died. Defendant did not testify at trial. The State called a detective, the officer who investigated the victim's murder, as a witness. Defendant acknowledged that his exculpatory statements to the detective were hearsay, but he argued that the trial court violated his right to a fair trial by excluding those statements when it allowed three other witnesses to testify as to incriminating statements that he made to them regarding the murder. The conflicting statements in defendant's case were made in different contexts to different people at different times. Moreover, the State did not attempt to offer any evidence relating to the detective's interrogation of defendant. The evidence was an attempt to offer the exculpatory hearsay statements by defendant into evidence without allowing the State an opportunity to cross-examine defendant with regard to those statements. Also, defendant only attempted to offer his exculpatory statements in order to counter the impact of incriminating statements made by the other witnesses at the trial. Thus, the trial court did not err when it excluded the exculpatory statements defendant made to the detective.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's conviction.

State v. Hernandez


No. 94,295


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 74; 159 P.3d 950; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 330



June 8, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hernandez v. Kan., 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12142 (U.S., Nov. 13, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Montgomery district court, ROGER L. GOSSARD, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Montgomery District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of first-degree murder and imposed a hard 40 sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his first-degree murder conviction and hard 40 sentence, but the supreme court affirmed, stating that although the excerpts from the prosecutor's closing argument constituted prosecutorial misconduct, the comments did not deny defendant a fair trial. When the prosecutor's comments were viewed in the context of the whole trial, the errors were harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The State's case against defendant did not turn on the fact that defendant remained silent after he was charged with the crime. Instead, it was based on a number of pieces of evidence, including the fact that his DNA matched that of the trail of blood drops found surrounding the victim's body and leading from her residence, that he provided inconsistent statements to the police officers, and that he had acknowledged his participation in the victim's murder to his wife. The admission of certain photographs were appropriate because each highlighted a different injury or a different aspect of an injury that the pathologist found relevant to the victim's cause and manner of death. The sentence was appropriate, in part because the victim was in "extreme fear" during the attack.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

254 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Redmond v. Kester


No. 97,627


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 209; 159 P.3d 1004; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 335



June 8, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification of question of law from the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit.
Redmond v. Kester (In re Kester), 339 B.R. 749, 2006 Bankr. LEXIS 319 (B.A.P. 10th Cir., 2006)



DISPOSITION: The answer to the certified question is determined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees debtors, a husband and wife, transferred ownership of their house to a revocable trust; when they filed for a Chapter 7 bankruptcy, they claimed a homestead exemption. The bankruptcy court denied appellant bankruptcy trustee's motion to compel the house be turned over to the bankruptcy estate. The United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit submitted a certified question regarding the homestead exemption.


OVERVIEW: The certified question asked whether a bankruptcy debtor could claim the homestead exemption allowed by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-2301 for real property that was placed in a self-settled living revocable trust prior to the bankruptcy, where the settlor and the beneficiary, as well as the bankruptcy debtor, were the same person. On review, the bankruptcy trustee asserted that the debtors could not claim the homestead exemption because the real estate was owned by a trust rather than the debtors. Disagreeing with the trustee, the supreme court answered yes to the certified question and concluded that a Chapter 7 bankruptcy debtor could claim the homestead exemption allowed by § 60-2301 even though the real property was transferred to a self-settled, living, revocable trust prior to the bankruptcy and the bankruptcy debtors were both the settlors and a beneficiaries of the trust. The supreme court broadly interpreted § 60-2301 to include occupants of real estate who held any type of interest, including an equitable interest in the real property.


OUTCOME: The supreme court answered yes to the certified question.

 

255 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re Comfort


No. 97,287


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 183; 159 P.3d 1011; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 337



June 8, 2007, Opinion Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Published censure.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner disciplinary administrator filed a disciplinary action against respondent attorney. A Hearing Panel of the Kansas Board for Discipline of Attorneys recommended a public censure.


OVERVIEW: When the attorney was representing a corporate client, he wrote letters to opposing counsel regarding a conflict of interest. Copies of one of the letters were sent to government officials. As a result, a disciplinary proceeding was instituted. A hearing panel recommended censure. In adopting this recommendation, the supreme court held that the attorney violated Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 4.4 because the publication of the letter was meant to embarrass opposing counsel. The attorney's subjective motive or purpose was relevant, but not determinative, in deciding whether Rule 4.4 had been violated. Next, Rule 4.4 was not limited only to protecting the rights of lay persons. Also, the attorney violated Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(d) by publishing the letter because it interfered a public records request and caused a loss of clients. Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(d) and Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 4.4 were not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. Since the attorney finally complied with Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 8.3 by reporting the conflict of interest, there was no violation of that rule. Finally, the recommended sanction was within the applicable guidelines, and it was consistent with prior precedent.


OUTCOME: The recommendation of a public censure was adopted.

State v. Johnson


No. 92,956


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


284 Kan. 18; 159 P.3d 161; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 328



June 8, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Johnson v. Kansas, 552 U.S. 1104, 128 S. Ct. 874, 169 L. Ed. 2d 737, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 394 (Jan. 7, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Johnson v. State, 247 P.3d 700, 2011 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 180 (Kan. Ct. App., Mar. 11, 2011)
Post-conviction relief denied at Johnson v. State, 309 P.3d 9, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 867 (Kan. Ct. App., Sept. 20, 2013)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Johnson v. State, 2017 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 720 (Kan. Ct. App., Sept. 1, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Wyandotte district court; J. DEXTER BURDETTE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) of one count of premeditated first-degree murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the district court erred by admitting photographs because they were overly repetitious and gruesome, only serving to inflame the jury. The supreme court disagreed. The photographs were admitted during the testimony of a doctor, who testified that the photographs would help illustrate his description of the injuries. There was no contention that the photographs showed unnecessary autopsy procedures, i.e., the gruesome nature of the photographs was caused by the gruesome manner in which the wounds were inflicted, not because the body was altered after the fact. There were a number of photographs, many of which appeared to depict more than one wound. However, the doctor testified that each photograph gave a clearer view of a particular wound, notwithstanding that other wounds might have been visible in the photograph. In addition, the district court did not err in denying admission of a police evidence custody receipt because the inconsistency in the document's contents negated any trustworthiness emanating from its preparation in the regular course of business.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Casco v. Armour Swift-Eckrich


No. 93,984


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


283 Kan. 508; 154 P.3d 494; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 235



 March 23, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 34 Kan. App. 2d 670, 128 P.3d 401 (2005). Appeal from Workers Compensation Board.
Casco v. Armour Swift-Eckrich, 34 Kan. App. 2d 670, 128 P.3d 401, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 1223 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the Workers Compensation Board is affirmed. Judgment of the Workers Compensation Board is reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kansas, which reversed a decision of the Workers Compensation Board concluding that appellee employee suffered a new and separate accident to his right shoulder due to repetitive use and reducing the amount the employee was awarded by an administrative law judge (ALJ).


OVERVIEW: The employee suffered a repetitive use injury to his left shoulder due to his employment. Because of the injury to his left shoulder and the restrictions associated with his treatment, the employee began using only his right arm to perform all of his job duties, and as a result he began experiencing pain in his right shoulder. On appeal of the appellate court's decision, the court held that the Board erroneously interpreted and applied the secondary injury rule, as it placed undue emphasis on a physician's testimony regarding the employee's work activities without considering the physician's testimony linking the injury to the employee's right shoulder with the injury to his left shoulder. The record contained uncontrovertible evidence establishing that the injury to the right shoulder was caused by overcompensating for the injury to his left shoulder. The court further held that the disability in both of the employee's arms was the result of a single injury, and because the employee suffered from the loss of both arms, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-510c(a)(2) established a rebuttable presumption that he was permanently, totally disabled.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing the decision of the Board was affirmed. The case was remanded to the ALJ to determine whether the employee was capable of engaging in any type of substantial and gainful employment.

 

258 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Flores Rentals, Inc. v. Flores


No. 95,535


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


283 Kan. 476; 153 P.3d 523; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 140



March 16, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Geary district court; BENJAMIN J. SEXTON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant rental business sought review of a decision of the Geary County District Court (Kansas), which granted the motion of appellee former partner to disqualify a law firm from serving as counsel for the business or its owner. The issue on appeal was whether the trial court's disqualification order was appealable pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-2102(a)(4) under the collateral order doctrine.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the former partner had a substantial relationship with the law firm and that disqualification was necessary under Kan. R. Prof. Conduct 1.9(a). The business unsuccessfully applied, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-2101(b), for permission to take an interlocutory appeal. It then filed a notice of appeal; the state appellate court ordered the parties to address whether Skahan v. Powell, applying the collateral order doctrine to retain the appeal of an order disqualifying counsel, should be reconsidered, and whether Parker v. Volswagenwerk Aktiengesellschaft, suggesting that an interlocutory appeal was the only immediate remedy for the business, applied to prevent an appeal. The case was transferred to the state supreme court, and it dismissed. The disqualification motion arose because the attorney might be a witness, meaning that the merits of the motion were inextricably intertwined with the merits of the case. Thus, there was no appellate jurisdiction under the collateral order doctrine. The reasoning from a U.S. Supreme Court opinion applied, which rejected application of the doctrine under 28 U.S.C.S. § 1291 in the context of disqualification orders.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction.

 

259 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fisher


No. 89,300


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


283 Kan. 272; 154 P.3d 455; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 142



March 16, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed February 11, 2005. Appeal from Pottawatomie district court; TRACY D. KLINGINSMITH, judge.
State v. Fisher, 105 P.3d 742, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 123 (Kan. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming in part the district court and remanding for resentencing is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed in part, sentence is vacated, and case is remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of unlawful manufacture of methamphetamine, possession of ephedrine with the intent to manufacture methamphetamine, possession of anhydrous ammonia in an unapproved container for the production of methamphetamine, possession of methamphetamine, and possession of paraphernalia for use in manufacturing methamphetamine. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions but remanded for resentencing. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying his motion to suppress evidence because the facts upon which the search warrant was based were insufficient to form the basis of probable cause. The supreme court disagreed. An officer noted a smell of ether, a substance used in the manufacture of methamphetamine. The officer noticed yellow bottles in a trash bag that looked like bottles usually containing methanol, which could be an ingredient in the manufacture of methamphetamine. The bottles' identification by the officer - who at virtually the identical location, i.e., the barn, hours earlier had noticed the strong smell of ether - was a circumstance to be considered in the probable cause analysis. A concerned citizen's notice of a peculiar smell in burning trash was in some measure corroborated by the strong smell of ether on the premises. The corroboration of part of the citizen's story suggested the reliability of the remainder of the officer's personally sensed information, i.e., the observation of vehicles coming and going and staying for a short time at the residence. Therefore, probable cause existed for the issuance of the search warrant.


OUTCOME: The sentences for unlawful manufacture of methamphetamine and possession of ephedrine should be vacated, and the matter should be remanded for resentencing. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

260 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Allen


No. 93,940


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


283 Kan. 372; 153 P.3d 488; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 145



March 16, 2007, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 35 Kan. App. 2d 466, 131 P.3d 1241 (2006). Appeal from Sedgwick district court, TERRY L. PULLMAN, judge.
State v. Allen, 35 Kan. App. 2d 466, 131 P.3d 1241, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 334 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is reversed, the sentence is vacated, and the case is remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of indecent liberties with a child and sentenced as a persistent sex offender to double the maximum duration of the presumptive term of imprisonment for his offense under the provisions of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4704(j)(1) (Supp. 2004). The Kansas Court of Appeals reversed defendant's enhanced sentence. The parties cross-petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a no contest plea as part of a plea agreement. The presentence investigation report (PSI report) revealed that defendant had a juvenile adjudication of aggravated incest in 1987. The PSI report labeled defendant a "persistent sex offender" under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4704(j) (Supp. 2004). The question on appeal was whether the increased sentence that was imposed ran afoul of the Apprendi and Gould protections. The supreme court concluded that it did not. Other than the fact of a prior conviction, any fact that increased the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum was required to be submitted to a jury and proved beyond a reasonable doubt. Here, the district court simply used the fact that defendant a prior conviction to impose an extended sentence. In doing so, the sentencing court was not required to make an additional finding of fact beyond that made by the jury. Despite the trial court's classification of defendant as a "persistent sex offender," a finding still needed to be made beyond a reasonable doubt that the 1987 juvenile adjudication for aggravated incest was sexually motivated under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3717(d)(2)(L) (Supp. 2004).


OUTCOME: The sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for imposition of sentence.

State v. Jones


No. 93,643


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


283 Kan. 186; 151 P.3d 22; 2007 Kan. LEXIS 25



February 9, 2007, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Jones v. State, 228 P.3d 441, 2010 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 282 (Kan. Ct. App., Apr. 15, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; THOMAS L. BOEDING, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) convicting him of one count of first-degree premeditated murder and one count of second-degree intentional murder and sentencing him to a hard 50 life sentence and 195 months in prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for shooting the victims to death after an argument. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion to suppress statements he made to law enforcement officers in a district attorney's conference room and all subsequent statements. Defendant was not subjected to a custodial interrogation while meeting with the officers in the conference room to prepare to testify in another suspect's prosecution for the murders, and therefore Miranda warnings were not required, because a reasonable person would have felt free to leave: (1) the other suspect remained the focus of the investigation and was on trial; (2) the police believed that there could be a noncriminal, plausible explanation for the presence of defendant's DNA on an electrical cord that was used to tie up one of the victims and as such, handled the interview as preparation for trial; (3) the officers told defendant that the interview was voluntary; and (4) defendant was never physically restrained or threatened. The Seibert case did not apply to defendant's subsequent statements because he was not in police custody at the time the officers questioned him.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentence were affirmed.

Bruch v. Kan. Dep't of Revenue


No. 95,029


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 764; 148 P.3d 538; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 735



December 22, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Reno district court, TIMOTHY J. CHAMBERS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After being arrested for driving under the influence (DUI), appellant driver's driving privileges were suspended by appellee Kansas Department of Revenue pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-259 following an administrative hearing. The driver filed a petition for review with the Reno County District Court (Kansas). A motion to dismiss was granted for failure to comply with the pleading requirements set forth in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-614(b).


OVERVIEW: The driver argued the district court erred in finding it did not have subject matter jurisdiction to conduct a trial de novo under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-259 because the petition for review failed to meet the requirements of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-614. The heart of the driver's attempted appeal involved the preliminary breath test (PBT) administered to him after he was stopped by a police officer. He asserted the PBT was inadmissible and provided no basis for probable cause to warrant the officer's request for a test under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1001(a) (2005) or for ultimate suspension of his license. The court held that the Kansas pleading requirements of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-614(b) were intended to create a more manageable framework for reviewing agency actions and that this was best served by a specific pleading requirement, which would apprise both the court and the agency of the positions to be raised. Compliance with the specific language of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-614(b) was necessary to meet the strict compliance requirement. The driver had not demonstrated that the Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-259 de novo review process altered his petition for review being jurisdictional.


OUTCOME: The dismissal was affirmed.

 

263 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Kleypas


No. 90,650


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 560; 147 P.3d 1058; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 716



December 8, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Crawford district court, DONALD R. NOLAND, judge.
State v. Kleypas, 272 Kan. 894, 40 P.3d 139, 2001 Kan. LEXIS 953 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review in an interlocutory appeal of a decision of the Crawford District Court (Kansas), which concluded that evidence of stalking was not admissible and relevant during the death penalty phase of a capital murder case to establish that the killing was done in a heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder and was sentenced to death. The death sentence was overturned and the matter remanded for resentencing. After the matter was remanded, defendant filed a motion in limine to bar the testimony of many of the State's proposed witnesses. The State proposed to show that the crime was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4625(1), because defendant allegedly stalked the victim before killing her. The district court ruled as a matter of law that stalking was not relevant to the heinous, atrocious, or cruel aggravating factor in death penalty proceedings. The court determined initially that it had jurisdiction to hear the appeal under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3603, in part, because the ruling would substantially impact on the State's ability to prosecute the case. The court held that the fact that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4625(1) was not amended at the same time as the Hard 50 statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4636(2005) did not preclude consideration of stalking of the victim as a relevant factor to establish that the murder was committed in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the district court and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

264 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Haddock v. State


No. 93,500


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 475; 146 P.3d 187; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 698



November 9, 2006, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Post-conviction proceeding at, Motion for new trial denied by Haddock v. State, 2012 Kan. LEXIS 481 (Kan., Oct. 5, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court, JAMES FRANKLIN DAVIS, judge.
State v. Haddock, 257 Kan. 964, 897 P.2d 152, 1995 Kan. LEXIS 90 (1995)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. He appealed a judgment of the Johnson District Court (Kansas) denying his Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-1507 motion and his motions for DNA testing under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2512 (2005).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for killing the victim, his wife, who was found beaten to death and lying under a pile of wood in the garage of their home. On appeal, the court held that the district court erred by denying defendant's first motion seeking additional DNA testing on hair, glasses, and fingernail scrapings under § 21-2512(f)(3) because the district court improperly found that the DNA evidence was inconclusive, as: (1) the postconviction DNA testing clearly established that the hair in the victim's hand was consistent with a third-party female rather than defendant as suggested at trial; (2) the fingernail scrapings tested indicated a DNA consistent with the victim's DNA profile rather than defendants; and (3) the eyeglasses contained both the victim's DNA and extraneous DNA from another source that was inconsistent with defendant. The district court erred by denying defendant's second motion seeking additional DNA testing on shoes, a shirt, and slacks because it should not have found that as a result of defendant's refusal to submit to further DNA testing, no substantive issues regarding the most recent DNA testing was before the district court.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying defendant's motion was affirmed. However, the judgment denying defendant's motions for DNA testing were reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

State v. Gonzalez


No. 91,469


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 73; 145 P.3d 18; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 647



October 27, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Seward district court; T. KEITH WILSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Seward County District Court (Kansas) convicting him of premeditated first-degree murder in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(a), attempted first-degree murder in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-3301, 21-3401(a), and discharging a firearm at an occupied vehicle in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4219.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for shooting the two victims, killing one of them. Defendant claimed that he did so in self-defense. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by excluding defendant's psychologist's expert opinion that defendant was incompetent to stand trial on the ground that it was based on hearsay, namely defendant's California medical records. The medical records were not authenticated as required by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-460(o) because the letter from the legal custodian of the records contained no seal. Because the records were not qualified and admitted under an exception to the hearsay rule, the records remained inadmissible hearsay upon which the psychologist could not base her opinion under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-456(b). Admission of defendant's unredacted videotaped interview, during which defendant admitted to committing prior criminal acts, did not impair defendant's substantial rights because his conviction did not turn on whether he committed the prior acts, there was overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, and the trial court's limiting instruction limited the prejudicial effect of the erroneously admitted evidence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

 

266 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Garcia


No. 93,412


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 252; 144 P.3d 684; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 649



October 27, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; WILLIAM SIOUX WOOLLEY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Sedgwick district court (Kansas) that convicted him of 2 counts of first-degree murder and 1 count of intentional second-degree murder, and sentenced him to hard 50 sentences for each first-degree murder conviction and to 165 months in prison for the second-degree murder, to be served consecutively. Defendant claimed he was denied his right to a speedy trial under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3402.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed the victims and then dismembered and burned the bodies. The trial court had granted the State continuances in order to obtain DNA evidence from the FBI lab. On appeal, defendant claimed he was denied his right to a speedy trial under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3402. The court held that defendant's speedy trial right was not violated because the DNA evidence was unavailable within the meaning of § 22-3402 where the cost of obtaining it from a private lab soon enough to avoid a continuance of the trial was prohibitive, which made the State's conduct in seeking a continuance reasonable. The court rejected defendant's hearsay claims regarding the admission of certain statements by witnesses on the ground that the evidence was not offered for the truth of the matter and fit within the exception to the hearsay rule in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-460(d)(3). Also, any error in admitting the evidence was harmless in light of the overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt. The court rejected defendant's claim that the trial court erred by imposing the hard 50 sentence without first having a jury determine that aggravating circumstances existed beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment.

 

267 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rogers


No. 92,814


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 218; 144 P.3d 625; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 652



October 27, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; JOHN ANDERSON III.
State v. Rogers, 276 Kan. 497, 78 P.3d 793, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 583 (2003)
State v. Kunellis, 276 Kan. 461, 78 P.3d 776, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 580 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the Johnson district court (Kansas), a jury convicted defendant of burglary, theft, and two counts of felony murder based upon the theft. The case was remanded for a new trial. The second jury convicted defendant of two counts of felony murder under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(b) and one count of theft under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3701, and he received life imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State presented evidence that defendant stole motorcycles, drove against traffic during a police chase, and collided with another vehicle resulting in two deaths. The first jury found defendant guilty of felony murder for commission of a crime based upon an improperly defined theft by exertion of unauthorized control theory. In the first appeal, the appellate court remanded for retrial due to the improper theft by exertion theory. On remand, the jury instruction correctly limited theft to obtaining unauthorized control over property. In the second appeal, the appellate court determined that defendant's conviction of felony murder on remand did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause of the United States Constitution or Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 10 because the first jury's convictions for felony murder based upon the improperly-defined theory of theft by exerting unauthorized control did not represent acquittals for the remaining theories of felony murder, e.g., theft by obtaining unauthorized control or burglary. The same crime was charged and tried in both trials. Defendants' arguments regarding law of the case, transcribed testimony, and jury instructions also failed.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the convictions.

State v. Snow


No. 93,749


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 323; 144 P.3d 729; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 657



October 27, 2006, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Snow, 2008 Kan. App. LEXIS 171 (Kan. Ct. App., Nov. 14, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; STEVE LEBEN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, sentences vacated, and remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions and sentences by the Johnson district court (Kansas) for multiple counts of nonresidential burglary, theft, and criminal damage to property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, his brother, and a friend broke into various retail establishments and stole items. While the charges were pending, defendant was incarcerated for unrelated crimes. The court held that because the evidence was ambiguous, it was reasonable for the district court to believe that the phone monitor listened to the live phone conversation of defendant with his bail bondsman from the jail rather than a recording of the conversation. Also, because the Confrontation Clause did not grant defendant the right to confront himself, his right to confront witnesses against him was not violated by admitting the recording of the phone conversation. Although the district court erred by not redacting references of defendant's other bad acts from the recording, the error was harmless because such evidence was irrelevant. Also, although the prosecutor's comments were insulting, demeaning, and unprofessional, they did not rise to the level of misconduct required by Tosh to require reversal. However, the case was remanded for resentencing because there was no departure sentence to double under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4720(c)(3) (Supp. 2005).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and vacated defendant's sentences.

State v. Reed


No. 93,430


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 272; 144 P.3d 677; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 663



October 27, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Chautauqua district court; RAWLEY JUDD DENT II, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Chautauqua Count District Court (Kansas) convicting him of first-degree premeditated murder, attempted second-degree murder, and aggravated burglary and sentencing him to a hard 50 life sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for shooting the two victims, his wife and his daughter, which killed his wife and injured his daughter. On appeal, the court held that the denial of defendant's motion to change the judge under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 20-311d(a) (2005 Supp.) did not violate his due process rights because he failed to proved actual bias or prejudice. The fact that defendant was sentenced to a hard 50 life sentence did not prove that the judge was biased because the trial court's findings concerning the aggravating and mitigating factors was supported by the record. The district court did not err by admitting the victim's 911 call into evidence because: (1) the call corroborated the testimony of the dispatcher, the officer to whom defendant offered his confessions, and the second victim's testimony; (2) the audiotape captured defendant's demeanor at the time of the events; (3) it documented events and the duration of the incident; and (4) it was highly probative as to the essential element of premeditation.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

Montoy v. State


No. 92,032


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


282 Kan. 9; 138 P.3d 755; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 479



July 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bergman v. Kansas, 549 U.S. 1078, 127 S. Ct. 730, 166 L. Ed. 2d 562, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 9262 (U.S., 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.
Montoy v. State, 278 Kan. 769, 102 P.3d 1160, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 2 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State challenged a decision from the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which entered judgment for plaintiffs by determining that the Kansas School District Finance and Quality Performance Act (SDFQPA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-6405 et seq., was unconstitutional. The mandate was stayed, but plaintiffs filed a motion to show cause after 2006 Kan. Sess. Laws ch. 197 (S.B. 549) was passed.


OVERVIEW: The district court held that the SDFQPA was unconstitutional because it did not make suitable provision for the finance of public schools under Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6. The supreme court upheld this decision in part but stayed the mandate to allow for legislative action. S.B. 549 materially and fundamentally changed the way that education was funded in Kansas. Plaintiffs then filed a motion for a show cause order. In reaffirming the prior orders, the supreme court determined that the case was not moot because the case was in the compliance stage. Next, the constitutionality of 2006 Kan. Sess. Laws ch. 197 was not before the supreme court. The school finance system under review was not the same system reviewed in the previous cases. The sole inquiry before the supreme court was whether the legislation complied with its previous orders. This standard was met by S.B. 549. A remand of the case to the district court to allow an amended pleading to challenge the new funding scheme was not warranted. The case had been pending for several years; moreover, because the plan was for three years, it would have taken time to ascertain its full financial impact.


OUTCOME: The previous order of the supreme court was reaffirmed. Previously ordered stays were lifted. The appeal was dismissed, and the case was remanded to the district court with instructions to dismiss the pending case.

 

271 of 1638 DOCUMENTS



Hayes Sight & Sound, Inc. v. ONEOK, Inc.


No. 92,704


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 1287; 136 P.3d 428; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 371



June 16, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; JAMES R. FLEETWOOD, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff businesses were awarded compensatory and punitive damages by the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) in a negligence action against defendants, a corporation and its subsidiary. Defendants appealed the denial of their requests for setoff of subrogation claims and the amount of the punitive damages awards of $ 5,250,000 for the two consolidated cases.


OVERVIEW: The businesses were destroyed when natural gas migrated from underground storage caverns and ignited. Defendants owned and operated those storage caverns. On appeal, the court held that defendants were entitled to a setoff of damages in the amount equal to that they paid to the businesses' insurers in settlement of their subrogation claims. Because the settlement between the insurers and defendants extinguished the insurers' subrogation rights, thereafter the businesses no longer retained the right to recover damages for the benefit of the insurers. The punitive damages award of $ 5,250,000 did not violate due process because: (1) while the harm to the businesses was economic only, the potential for physical harm to their staff and customers was considerable; (2) defendants deviated from sound operating procedures in pumping gas; (3) the subsidiary's stonewalling after the explosion and fire did not reflect regard for the town's resident's safety; (4) the ratio of punitive to compensatory damages was three to one; and (5) a rough estimate of the sanctions imposed on defendants was half a million dollars. The award did not violate the statutory cap of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-3702(e).


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed insofar as defendants had been denied a setoff in the amount that they had paid the businesses' insurers in settlement of their subrogation claims. The punitive damages award was affirmed.


 

272 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Baker


No. 92,870


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 997; 135 P.3d 1098; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 362



June 9, 2006, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Baker v. State, 42 Kan. App. 2d 949, 219 P.3d 827, 2009 Kan. App. LEXIS 885 (2009)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Baker v. State, 2015 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 456 (Kan. Ct. App., June 5, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick County district court, ERIC YOST, judge.



DISPOSITION: Conviction affirmed, sentence vacated, and case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Sedgwick County District Court (Kansas) of first-degree premeditated murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his hard 50 sentence must be vacated because there was insufficient evidence to support the trial court's finding that the victim's murder was committed in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner. The supreme court agreed. Defendant shot the victim, a paraplegic. However, standing alone, the fact that a victim was killed by gunshots fired by a defendant was generally not sufficient to support a finding that the manner of death was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel. There might have been some factual circumstances in which a defendant subjected a defenseless victim to mental anguish because the victim was conscious and aware that death was imminent but completely unable, by virtue of his or her physical condition, to defend him or herself. However, the facts of the case did not warrant such a finding. Any finding that defendant taunted, threatened, or terrorized the victim with the gun or that the victim knew he was going to die prior to being shot required speculation about facts that were not in evidence.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed. The sentence was vacated and the case was remanded for resentencing.

Ann. § 21-4635(b) (2005 Supp.), during the sentencing hearing.

Post-conviction relief dismissed at Kirtdoll v. State, 293 P.3d 816, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 111 (Kan. Ct. App., Feb. 8, 2013)
Post-conviction relief denied at Kirtdoll v. State, 306 Kan. 335, 393 P.3d 1053, 2017 Kan. LEXIS 144 (May 12, 2017)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Kirtdoll v. Heimgartner, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 180012 (D. Kan., Oct. 31, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; CHARLES E. ANDREWS, Jr., judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Shawnee District Court (Kansas) found defendant guilty of premeditated first-degree murder and two counts of aggravated battery for his involvement in a nightclub shooting. The trial court sentenced him to life in prison without the possibility of parole for 50 years for the first-degree murder conviction and two 41-month sentences for the aggravated battery convictions. The sentences were ordered to run consecutively. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: During an interrogation, defendant confirmed three aliases. A detective then asked him if he wanted to speak about the shooting incident at a dance club. Defendant declined because he had no lawyer present. The detective confirmed that he wanted an attorney present before speaking further with the detective, and the interview terminated. Substantial competent evidence supported the trial court's findings which permitted the supreme court to independently concluded that, under the totality of the circumstances, defendant's Miranda rights were knowingly and voluntarily waived and his statement to a detective was voluntarily and knowingly given. Regarding the duration and manner of the interrogation and his ability on request to communicate with the outside world, the interview itself was short, as defendant invoked his right to counsel after answering the detective's first questions. Also, regarding his age, intellect, and background, defendant was a 26-year-old adult who had previous experience with law enforcement. In addition, defendant had been Mirandized. Thus, there was no allegation of low intellect or of the detective's unfairness in conducting the interrogation.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Kempke v. Kan. Dep't of Revenue


No. 94,013


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 770; 133 P.3d 104; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 248



May 5, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Ellsworth district court, BARRY A. BENNINGTON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Kansas Department of Revenue (KDR) challenged a decision from the Ellsworth District Court (Kansas), which determined that the statutory scheme of the Kansas implied consent law, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1001, was unconstitutional as applied to appellee licensee under the Fourteenth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: In an appeal from an administrative hearing, the licensee contended that he was denied due process of law because he was not allowed to subpoena relevant witnesses to testify at the hearing relating to a license suspension. The district court agreed, and the KDR sought review. In reversing, the appellate court determined that the Kansas implied consent law was not unconstitutional as applied to the facts of the case. Although Wulfkuhle and Carson had required the subpoena of relevant witnesses at an initial administrative hearing in a licensee's license suspension case, the Kansas implied consent law underwent numerous changes after those cases. This included an extensive hearing at the administrative level and an automatic extension of driving privileges if the licensee filed a petition for review in a district court, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1020. By appealing to the district court, subject to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1014, the licensee had a full due process hearing before any final action was taken against his driving privileges. The licensee was provided with a meaningful hearing and the right to call and cross-examine witnesses before any final action was taken.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

State v. Bunyard


No. 88,546


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 392; 133 P.3d 14; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 215; 33 A.L.R.6th 751



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 31 Kan. App. 2d 853, 75 P.3d 750 (2003). Appeal from Sedgwick district court; JAMES FLEETWOOD and REBECCA L. PILSHAW, judges.
State v. Bunyard, 31 Kan. App. 2d 853, 75 P.3d 750, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 757 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed and the case is remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kansas affirming his conviction of one count of rape in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3502(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of raping the victim at a party. On appeal, the court held that the prosecutor committed misconduct by misstating the law during closing arguments by equating the "overcome by force or fear" element of rape with the act of sexual intercourse/penetration, stating that the force of defendant's penis into the victim's vagina satisfied the force element of the rape charge. This error was not harmless, and therefore defendant was entitled to a new trial, because the only guidance the jury was given as to what constituted the necessary force was the prosecutor's misstatement of the law; the jury instructions did not define the "overcome by force or fear" element of rape. The evidence against defendant regarding whether the victim was "overcome by force" was not so direct and overwhelming as to have little weight in the minds of the jurors. It was entirely possible under the facts that the jury could have concluded that defendant's continuance of intercourse after the victim withdrew her consent was sufficient to establish rape without finding that the victim was overcome by force or fear.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new trial.

 

278 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Elliott


No. 92,853


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 583; 133 P.3d 1253; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 223



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed August 12, 2005. Appeal from Johnson district court; STEPHEN R. TATUM, judge.
State v. Elliott, 117 P.3d 150, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 787 (Kan. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving with a suspended license and driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI) with two or more prior DUI convictions, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567 (Supp. 2005). The court granted the State's petition for review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which affirmed a judgment excluding two of defendant's four prior municipal court DUI convictions for criminal history scoring purposes.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty. The presentence investigation report revealed that defendant had five prior DUI convictions, all committed within a 5-year period. The first three prior DUI convictions occurred in October 1994 in municipal court and were listed as misdemeanors. The fourth prior DUI conviction occurred in February 1996 in municipal court and was also listed as a misdemeanor. The fifth prior DUI conviction, a felony, occurred in September 1998 in district court. The district court struck two of defendant's prior DUI convictions from his criminal history score, convicted him of felony DUI as a fourth-time offender under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567(g), and sentenced him to 9 months in jail. The sole issue raised by the State on appeal was whether the district court erred in excluding two of defendant's prior convictions on the ground that the municipal court lacked subject matter jurisdiction. The court found that it did not err. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567 classified third and subsequent DUI convictions as felonies. Thus, municipalities could not prosecute third or subsequent DUI violations because they were statutorily designated as felonies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals affirming the district court judgment.

 

279 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Schoonover


No. 90,360


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 453; 133 P.3d 48; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 229



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed November 5, 2004. Appeal from McPherson district court; CARL B. ANDERSON, JR., judge.
State v. Schoonover, 99 P.3d 1152, 2004 Kan. App. LEXIS 1141 (Kan. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court on the issues subject to our grant of review is affirmed. Judgment of the district court on these issues is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which affirmed his convictions on seven counts arising from the manufacture and possession of methamphetamine under Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 65-4159 and 65-4160.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found passed out in a vehicle and was arrested for having an open container. The vehicle in which defendant was sitting was described as a "rolling meth lab." In applying the Blockburger test, the court found that while some of the process, such as gathering paraphernalia, could have occurred at another location, the actions were part of one transaction of manufacturing methamphetamine, and the convictions arose from unitary conduct. Because defendant was convicted under multiple statutes, the same-elements test was the applied in determining whether there was a double jeopardy violation. Because manufacturing methamphetamine required proof of manufacturing, while possession of methamphetamine did not, and possession of methamphetamine required proof of possession, while manufacturing did not, there was not an identity of elements between the two statutes, and no double jeopardy violation. Further, the elements of manufacturing methamphetamine and possession of the ingredients had different elements as well. Therefore, the convictions did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause of the Fifth Amendment or Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 10.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.
