Hawley v. Kan. Dep't of Agric.


No. 93,690


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 603; 132 P.3d 870; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 230



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Republic district court; THOMAS M. TUGGLE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state division of water resources (DWR) sought review of an order of the Republic County District Court (Kansas) setting aside DWR's termination of a water right under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 82a-718 (Supp. 2005), owned by appellee trustees.


OVERVIEW: The water permit was issued for irrigation in 1953. Letters were sent to the previous owners, then to the trustees after the previous owners passed away regarding abandonment of the water right due to non-use. The district court found that DWR had not complied with the notice requirements of § 82a-718(b). The court reversed and held that the language "shall be deemed abandoned and shall terminate" clearly meant that by operation of law those water rights shall terminate, regardless of a party's intent, and that the Legislature created an act of forfeiture. The Kansas Water Appropriation Act created a rule that required holders to undertake minimal acts indicative of ownership at least once every 5 years, and it was clear that there was no due and sufficient cause for nonuse from 1971 through 2001. The hearing officer correctly held that because nonuse had already occurred for more than 30 consecutive years, § 82a-718(b)'s notice after 3 years of nonuse advising of one's opportunity to avoid termination through use, or excused nonuse, during a 5-year period simply did not apply. DWR's interpretation of the statute was correct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court and affirmed the decision of DWR terminating the water right.


 

281 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Bd. of County Comm'rs v. Whitson


No. 95,193


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 678; 132 P.3d 920; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 232



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Leavenworth district court; DAVID J. KING, judge.
Kansas v. Hendricks, 521 U.S. 346, 117 S. Ct. 2072, 138 L. Ed. 2d 501, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3999 (1997)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant operators challenged a decision from Leavenworth District Court (Kansas), which granted appellee county board's request for a permanent injunction preventing the operators from having a group home for transitioning sexually violent predators without obtaining a special use permit.


OVERVIEW: When a sex offender sought to move to the operators' facility, a temporary injunction was granted because the operators had no license. After that problem was corrected, county amended its zoning and subdivision regulations to require a special use permit for certain other types of uses. The district court then granted a permanent injunction, and the operators sought review. In affirming, the appellate court determined that there was no requirement to show proof of certainty of irreparable harm when applying for injunctive relief. Only a reasonable probability of irreparable future injury was required. This standard was met because the sex offender was likely to reoffend, despite his reduced risk due to poor health, advanced age, and the safeguards of the facility. Next, the facility in question was a "group home" under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 12-736(b)(1), and the sex offender was a person with a disability under § 12-736(b)(3). However, the county was free to impose restrictions or completely exclude group homes for disabled persons with additional characteristics set out in § 12-736(c), and this included disabled transitioning sexually violent predators.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

282 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Robinson


No. 91,875


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 538; 132 P.3d 934; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 212



April 28, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 33 Kan. App. 2d 773, 109 P.3d 185 (2005). Appeal from Saline district court; DAN D. BOYER, judge.
State v. Robinson, 33 Kan. App. 2d 773, 109 P.3d 185, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 288 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court on the issue subject to our grant of review is reversed. Judgment of the district court on this issue is reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of felony possession of marijuana and misdemeanor possession of drug paraphernalia. The district court assessed attorney and administrative fees to reimburse the State Board of Indigents' Defense Services (BIDS). The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was ordered to pay restitution, court costs, a probation fee, and other fees that included attorney fees and a BIDS administrative fee. On appeal, defendant argued that the sentencing judge violated Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4513 (Supp. 2005). More specifically, the supreme court was called upon to answer two questions: (1) whether such an assessment under the statute was error if a sentencing judge failed to explicitly consider sua sponte at the time of assessment a defendant's ability to pay and the financial burden payment would impose, and (2) whether such an assessment violated a defendant's due process rights if the sentencing judge failed to consider the validity of the fees at the time of assessment. The supreme court determined that the sentencing court, at the time of initial assessment, had to consider defendant's financial resources. Nevertheless, a sentencing court's failure to include such explicit consideration in the record did not render the sentence associated with the resulting assessment "illegal," as that term was used in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3504. Finally, as to defendant's due process claims, he received both notice and an opportunity to be heard.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed. The judgment of the district court was reversed, and the matter was remanded with directions.

State v. Hill


No. 89,572


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 136; 130 P.3d 1; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 138

DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed on the single issue subject to our review. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Saline District Court (Kansas), which denied his motion to suppress and convicted him of conspiracy to manufacture methamphetamine, manufacturing methamphetamine, and possession of ephedrine. The appellate court had affirmed the convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions, which were previously affirmed by the appellate court. The supreme court granted defendant's petition for review to consider his claim that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence obtained by the police after his arrest without probable cause. The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's decision on other grounds. The court concluded that defendant was arrested without probable cause and illegally detained without reasonable suspicion in violation of the Fourth Amendment and Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15. However, that determination did not automatically preclude him from being prosecuted. The court affirmed defendant's convictions because the error in admitting the evidence at issue was harmless. Beyond a reasonable doubt, the erroneous admission of his statements and the key from his pocket could not have affected the trial. At trial, two codefendants testified against defendant and the trial court did not rely on the evidence when the judge found defendant guilty.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was affirmed on other grounds.

 

284 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gayden


No. 94,651


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


281 Kan. 290; 130 P.3d 108; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 143



March 17, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Lyon district court, MERLIN G. WHEELER, judge.
State v. Gayden, 259 Kan. 69, 910 P.2d 826, 1996 Kan. LEXIS 5 (1996)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Lyon District Court (Kansas), which denied his pro se motion to correct an illegal sentence under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3504(1). Defendant was sentenced to consecutive sentences totaling life plus 68 months in prison and 1 year in jail. Defendant asserted that his felony convictions involving personal injury arose from a single wrongful act and cumulative punishments were therefore barred by double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: In a crowded bar, a dispute broke out between defendant and another man. Defendant fired numerous shots at the other man, hitting several bystanders. Defendant was convicted of first-degree felony murder, attempted voluntary manslaughter, four counts of reckless aggravated battery, a firearms violation, and possession of cocaine. On appeal, the court noted that before consideration of the issue on its merits, the court first had to determine whether a claimed constitutional double jeopardy violation for imposing consecutive sentences fit within the definition of "illegal sentence" as contemplated by § 22-3504(1). The court held that it did not. To meet the definition of an illegal sentence, the claim had to be that the term of the sentence did not conform to relevant statutory provisions. Defendant made no such claim. Rather, defendant's allegation was that his sentence violated a constitutional provision, U.S. Const. amend. V. The court concluded that the district court properly denied relief on the motion to correct an illegal sentence but did so based on the wrong reason. Relief should have been denied because the claim did not fit within the definition of an illegal sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

285 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brown


No. 92,910


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 898; 127 P.3d 257; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 10



February 3, 2006, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; J. DEXTER BURDETTE, judge.
State v. Brown, 266 Kan. 563, 973 P.2d 773, 1999 Kan. LEXIS 13 (1999)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree premeditated murder in 1996 and sentenced to life in prison with no possibility of parole for 25 years, and his conviction was affirmed on appeal. Defendant filed a motion to correct an illegal sentence, alleging that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401 was unconstitutional. The Wyandotte District Court, Kansas, denied the motion, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that his sentence was illegal because Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401 was unconstitutionally vague because the legislature eliminated the word "malice" from the statute; (2) the absence of the word "malice" shifted the burden of proof to defendant; and (3) the trial court should have instructed the jury regarding the element of malice. The appellate court affirmed the constitutionality of the statute and ruled: (1) the State had previously affirmed that the removal of the term "malicious" for second-degree murder in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3402 did not render that statute vague, (2) removal of the term malice did not shift the burden of proof to defendant, (3) defendant did not preserve for review his claim that his conviction should be reversed because the trial court improperly denied his request for a jury instruction regarding the element of malice, and (4) defendant's claim that the trial court's failure to improperly instruct the jury resulted in a trial error was also rejected, because a trial error did not cause the trial court to lose jurisdiction, and thus, the issue could not be raised in a motion to correct an illegal sentence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's denial of defendant's motion to correct an illegal sentence.

Alpha Med. Clinic v. Anderson


No. 93,383


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 903; 128 P.3d 364; 2006 Kan. LEXIS 18



February 3, 2006, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Comprehensive Health of Planned Parenthood of Kan. & Mid-Missouri, Inc. v. Kline, 287 Kan. 372, 197 P.3d 370, 2008 Kan. LEXIS 701 (2008)
Related proceeding at In re Kline, 298 Kan. 96, 311 P.3d 321, 2013 Kan. LEXIS 1144 (2013)
Related proceeding at, Dismissed by, in part, Dismissed without prejudice by, in part Kline v. Biles, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 158229 (D. Kan., Nov. 14, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original action in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Writ of mandamus granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: As part of an inquisition against petitioner medical clinics on allegations of unjustified late-term abortions and possible unreported child sexual abuse, respondent state attorney general subpoenaed unredacted patient files from the clinics. The clinics filed a motion to quash the subpoenas. Respondent judge orally denied the motion to quash but later ordered a stay of the production of the subpoenaed files. The clinics sought mandamus relief.


OVERVIEW: In their petition for mandamus relief, the clinics requested that the judge and the attorney general be compelled to cease further enforcement of the subpoenas. The supreme court determined that the Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3101(1) (Supp. 2004) judicial inquisition in this case permitted subpoenas calling for the production of documents as well as subpoenas calling for witness testimony; it held that the standard to be employed by a district judge evaluating whether to issue such subpoenas or documents was reasonable suspicion rather than probable cause. After weighing the competing interests, including the type of information requested, the potential harm in disclosure, the adequacy of safeguards to prevent unauthorized disclosure, and the need for access, the supreme court held that mandamus was warranted as the judge's order did not do all it could to narrow the information gathered to safeguard that information from unauthorized disclosure. While the supreme court agreed with the clinics that the attorney general should not have released some material to the press, the supreme court did not hold him in contempt as no prejudice resulted from his conduct.


OUTCOME: The medical clinics' petition for writ of mandamus was granted.

State v. Rupnick


No. 92,193


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 720; 125 P.3d 541; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 876



December 16, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Brown district court; JOHN L. WEINGART, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Brown District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of felony computer crime in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3755(b)(1)(C) (2004 Supp.), and misdemeanor computer trespass.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with three violations of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3755(b)(1)(C) (2004 Supp.), felony computer crime. The jury found him guilty of lesser included misdemeanor computer trespass under § 21-3755(d) on Counts I and II and guilty as charged on Count III. On appeal, the court stated that the warrantless seizure of defendant's computer was excused by the exception for probable cause plus exigent circumstances. The district court correctly denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence from the laptop, insofar as the motion was based on that seizure. However, the court reversed the conviction on Count III, stating that the search warrant was invalid and could not have supported the search conducted on defendant's laptop. Its execution outside the jurisdiction designated by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-2503 (2004 Supp.) was not a mere technical irregularity. It affected the substantial rights of the defendant, enabling his conviction on a felony. His conviction of felony computer crime on Count III must be reversed and the case remanded for further proceedings on that charge.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for felony computer crime was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings on that charge.

State v. Oliver


No. 88,987


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 681; 124 P.3d 493; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 874



December 16, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Oliver v. Kansas, 547 U.S. 1183, 126 S. Ct. 2361, 165 L. Ed. 2d 286, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 4421 (2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Oliver v. State, 301 P.3d 789, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 457 (Kan. Ct. App., 2013)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Motion denied by Oliver v. Cline, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 57794 (D. Kan., Apr. 25, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; JOSEPH BRIBIESCA, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) that convicted him on two counts of first-degree premeditated murder and two counts of first-degree felony murder and that imposed two consecutive hard 50 sentences on the first-degree premeditated murder convictions and to two consecutive life sentences on the two felony-murder convictions for the shooting deaths of his ex-girlfriend and three other victims.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed the trial court erred in denying his suppression motion and in limiting testimony about his psychological disorders. The court held that the suppression motion was properly denied because police had probable cause to support a warrantless arrest where the evidence showed defendant had violent relationship with his ex-girlfriend, including threats to kill her with a gun and anyone who helped her; he had stolen her car; one victim was in a rival gang; and matching gun shells were found at the scene. The court found that the evidence was insufficient to warrant an instruction on compulsion or the lesser-included offenses of capital and premeditated murder. The court rejected defendant's claim that the hard 50 sentencing statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4635, was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment.

 

289 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Walker


No. 91,271


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 513; 124 P.3d 39; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 850



December 9, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed January 14, 2005. Appeal from Reno district court, RICHARD J. ROME, judge.
State v. Walker, 103 P.3d 993, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 55 (Kan. Ct. App., 2005).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Kansas) affirmed the trial court, which designated defendant's severity level two crime as the primary crime. Defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: The question presented was whether Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4720(b)(2) (2004 Supp.) required a sentencing court, when sentencing a defendant for multiple felonies, to designate defendant's severity level one crime, which was sentenced as a severity level four crime, as the primary crime. Defendant pled no contest to the following drug felonies: possession of ephedrine with intent to manufacture, severity level one; possession of methamphetamine with intent to sell within 1,000 feet of a school, severity level two; possession of methamphetamine, severity level four; and possession of drug paraphernalia with intent to manufacture, severity level four. The sentencing court utilized the presumptive range for a severity level four drug felony, 10-12 months, rather than the range for a severity level one drug felony, 142-161 months. The trial court designated the severity level two felony as the primary crime, which carried a presumptive range of 49-54 months. The primary crime had to be the crime with the highest severity level ranking which was actually sentenced. Thus, the trial court's designation of the primary crime under the facts of this case was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Thomas


No. 91,437


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 526; 124 P.3d 48; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 851; 17 A.L.R.6th 813



December 9, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed February 11, 2005. Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.
State v. Thomas, 105 P.3d 742, 2005 Kan. App. LEXIS 128 (Kan. Ct. App., 2005).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of unlawfully manufacturing a controlled substance, illegal possession of pseudoephedrine, and possession of methamphetamine with intent to sell. Defendant appealed the judgment of the Kansas Court of Appeals, which affirmed a district court judgment denying defendant's motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: Police received information that the suspect, wanted on a felony arrest warrant, was trying to sell an informant anhydrous ammonia. Police began surveillance on the house where the informant indicated the suspect might be found. When the suspect stepped out of the house, the officers ordered him to stop, but the suspect ran into the residence. The officers pursued the suspect into the house and arrested him. While inside and in plain view, the officers observed various items that they believed were consistent with a methamphetamine lab. The officers discovered six other individuals inside the house, including defendant, who owned the residence. The court found that the officers were justified in making a warrantless entry into defendant's residence in the hot pursuit of the suspect and in seizing evidence that was in plain view. The officers observed the suspect run into the house and clearly had probable cause to believe that the suspect was inside. The officers were not prohibited from entering the house in hot pursuit of the suspect, even though the suspect did not own or reside in the house, and even though the officers did not have a search warrant for the house.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals affirming the district court was affirmed, and the judgment of the district court was affirmed.

State v. Washington


No. 92,438


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 565; 123 P.3d 1265; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 846



December 9, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Washington v. Kansas, 549 U.S. 1018, 127 S. Ct. 552, 166 L. Ed. 2d 408, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8380 (2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Washington v. State, 216 P.3d 191, 2009 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 758 (Kan. Ct. App., 2009)
Post-conviction relief denied at Washington v. State, 311 P.3d 1168, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1002 (Kan. Ct. App., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court, JOHN J. MCNALLY, judge.
State v. Washington, 275 Kan. 644, 68 P.3d 134, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 279 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's first-degree premeditated murder and criminal possession of a firearm convictions were affirmed on appeal, but the sentence was remanded for resentencing. On remand, the Wyandotte District Court, Kansas, found the mitigating factors involving posttraumatic stress disorder did not negate defendant's premeditation and intent. A hard 50 sentence and 18-month sentence for the respective crimes were imposed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant argued that the evidence failed to support the trial court's determination that the crime was committed in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner. The supreme court disagreed found that the sole distinguishing factor weighing against the aggravating circumstance was that the shootings took place within a short period of time, approximately 2 minutes, and that the victim was pronounced dead within approximately 25 minutes of the shooting. Nevertheless, it was clear that the victim was chased and suffered substantial mental anguish as demonstrated by her attempt to escape on one foot and her repeated cries for help before she was killed. Further, the trial judge carefully considered the mitigating factors regarding defendant's posttraumatic stress disorder but made a credibility determination between competing expert opinions regarding the effect of defendant's mental state on his actions. The court did not reject these factors outright, but rather concluded that they were not entitled to much weight based upon the evidence presented at trial. As such, the trial court did not err in imposing a hard 50 sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

292 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Limon


No. 85,898


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 275; 122 P.3d 22; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 715



October 21, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at State v. Limon, 2007 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 732 (Kan. Ct. App., Apr. 6, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 32 Kan. App. 2d 369, 83 P.3d 229 (2004). Appeal from Miami district court; RICHARD M. SMITH, judge.
State v. Limon, 32 Kan. App. 2d 369, 83 P.3d 229, 2004 Kan. App. LEXIS 110 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of criminal sodomy under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3505(a)(2), based on a stipulation that when he was 18 years old he engaged in consensual oral contact with a 14-year-old individual of the same sex. After announcing its Lawrence decision, the U.S. Supreme Court vacated the judgment and remanded the case to the Kansas Court of Appeals, which then rejected defendant's equal protection argument. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3522 (Supp. 2004), the unlawful voluntary sexual relations statute, provided for less severe punishment than the sodomy statute in cases, like the one at bar, where a defendant was under 19 and less than 4 years older than a 14-year-old victim. However, the statute, commonly referred to as "the Romeo and Juliet statute," applied only where the victim and offender were of the opposite sex. Defendant argued that § 21-3522 thus violated the Kansas and U.S. Equal Protection Clauses. The court agreed. The enormous escalation in the severity of punishment meant for defendant that he received a 206-month, rather than a 13-, 14-, or 15-month, prison sentence and that he had to register as a sex offender. Thus, there was a discriminatory classification. Under Lawrence, the court found no rational basis for that classification. Noting the link between the substantive-due-process and equal-protection analyses, the court followed the due process holding that moral disapproval of a group was not a legitimate governmental interest. The court could discern no relationship between the classification and the State's legitimate interests, including its interest in public health.


OUTCOME: The court struck from the statute the words "and are members of the opposite sex," reversed defendant's conviction and sentence for criminal sodomy, and remanded the cause with directions that the State be given 30 days in which to charge defendant under the statute without the stricken language or to take other action.

State v. Torres


No. 90,613


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 309; 121 P.3d 429; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 716



October 21, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Torres v. Roberts, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 41197 (D. Kan., June 4, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Wichita District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of the felony murder of his child in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-3436 and 21-3401 based on the underlying crime of felony abuse of a child in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-3436(a)(7) and 21-3609.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of the felony murder of his 21-month-old daughter, based on the underlying crime of felony abuse of a child. The maximum sentence of life imprisonment was imposed. On appeal, the supreme court affirmed, stating that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction. It was undisputed that defendant was the only person with the victim when she became unconscious and defendant admitted that he shook the child and threw her toward the bed but that she hit her head on a night stand. Additionally, expert testimony indicated that the victim's injuries were caused by both a shaking and an impact. Even defendant's own expert testified that although he felt there was no evidence that the victim was shaken, he believed that she died as a result of severe child abuse. The district court did not err in denying his motion to suppress his statements to law enforcement officials because the interviews were not custodial interrogations. Additionally, photographs of the victim were relevant and admissible because they were used to either prove the manner of death or to explain medical testimony, or both.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

294 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lackey


No. 90,532


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 190; 120 P.3d 332; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 466



September 30, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lackey v. Kansas, 547 U.S. 1056, 126 S. Ct. 1653, 164 L. Ed. 2d 399, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 2738 (2006)
Related proceeding at State v. Lackey, 2009 Kan. App. LEXIS 559 (Kan. Ct. App., June 12, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Saline district court, DAN D. BOYER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, sentence vacated, and case remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Saline District Court (Kansas) of premeditated first-degree murder and one count of rape. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the admission of a deceased cab driver's hearsay statement's violated his Confrontation rights as set out in Crawford. The supreme court disagreed. The testimony was important because it inferred that the cab driver picked up someone who matched defendant's general description from a convenience store near the victim's home and the man was rushed and disheveled. While the deceased driver was unable to testify, the defense was able to cross-examine an officer about the fact that he did not ask the driver if the fare had waited inside or outside in the light or shadows, about the number of fares he had driven that evening, how long his shift was, or if he had a second job, whether he had been drinking that evening, or whether he wore glasses. In that manner, the defense was able to attack the credibility of the testimony in some manner. Ultimately, defendant's convictions relied not upon a few lines of hearsay from a deceased cab driver but upon the overwhelming DNA evidence.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed and the sentence was vacated. The case was remanded for resentencing.

State v. Chamberlain


No. 91,007


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 241; 120 P.3d 319; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 472



September 30, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed September 3, 2004. Appeal from Johnson district court, JOHN ANDERSON, III, judge.
State v. Chamberlain, 96 P.3d 695, 2004 Kan. App. LEXIS 945 (Kan. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of his third driving under the influence (DUI) offense. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567(l)(3) worked as an unconstitutional impairment of his contractual rights under his 1986 DUI diversion agreement because the purpose of the contract was to avoid a conviction and the long-term legal consequences and the social stigma that flowed from a criminal record. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant avoided a criminal conviction and had no 1986 DUI conviction listed in his criminal history. He failed to recognize that a diversion or conviction in 1986 could be considered for sentencing enhancement at a later date. While the 1986 diversion provided defendant with those benefits when compared to a conviction, the diversion provided defendant no additional benefits over being convicted for purposes of future sentencing enhancement. Defendant did not establish the impairment of any obligation under his 1986 diversion agreement.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

296 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Engelhardt


No. 90,674


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 113; 119 P.3d 1148; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 462



September 16, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Engelhardt v. State, 246 P.3d 413, 2011 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 43 (Kan. Ct. App., 2011)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Engelhardt v. Heimgartner, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 11954 (D. Kan., Jan. 31, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Harvey district court; CARL B. ANDERSON, JR., judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Harvey district court (Arkansas) that convicted him of first-degree premeditated murder and imposed a "hard 50" life sentence after finding aggravating circumstances existed under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4636(a) and (f) (Supp. 2004) in that defendant was previously convicted of a felony in which he inflicted great bodily harm and the murder was committed in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised numerous claims of error on appeal. The court affirmed his conviction. The court held that the jury view of the crime scene did not result in a violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3418 because § 22-3418 stated that jurors "may be" accompanied by the defendant on a jury view but the defendant's presence was not required. The court also held that the jury view did not constitute a critical stage of the proceedings against defendant for purposes of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3405(1) or the Confrontation Clause and the Due Process Clause. The court found that defendant was not entitled to lesser included crime instructions because the overwhelming evidence demonstrated that he was guilty of either intentionally murdering the victim or aiding and abetting the intentional murder. Also, the court held that the trial court did not err in concluding that aggravating circumstances under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4636(f)(3) and (4) existed where the evidence showed that defendant and his accomplice stood over the victim and stabbed him approximately 55 times, the attack lasted about 20 minutes, and witnesses heard the victim's anguished screams.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment.

 

297 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jackson


No. 89,620


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


280 Kan. 16; 118 P.3d 1238; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 456



September 9, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Combs, 280 Kan. 45, 118 P.3d 1259, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 457 (2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jackson v. Kansas, 546 U.S. 1184, 126 S. Ct. 1363, 164 L. Ed. 2d 73, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 1350 (2006)
Subsequent appeal at Jackson v. State, 209 P.3d 764, 2009 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 472 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; JOHN J. McNALLY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) of First-degree premeditated murder, kidnapping, and conspiracy to commit murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his right to a speedy trial was violated. The supreme court disagreed. The trial court issued a 30-day continuance because the trial of a codefendant extended beyond the time previously allotted by the trial court. Although defendant did not object, he claimed that the continuance was the second 30-day continuance pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3402(3)(d), in violation of the statute. However, because the trial court's anticipatory note did not amount to a court order, the trial court did not erroneously grant two 30-day continuances. In addition, the State's efforts to procure the DNA evidence for trial were reasonable. Although the State could inform crime labs of trial deadlines and encourage them to process evidence as quickly as possible, it could not control the crime labs' schedules or case loads. The State was not responsible for the 119 backlogged cases that required DNA testing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

298 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Bland v. Scott


No. 89,773, No. 90,402


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 962; 112 P.3d 941; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 362



June 10, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Douglas district court; JACK A. MURPHY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, family of the decedent, filed suit in the Douglas District Court (Kansas) against defendants, various campus fraternity entities, alleging wrongful death after the decedent was killed in an automobile accident caused by a fraternity student who had been drinking at the fraternity. The court dismissed the suit. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by dismissing the suit for failure to state a claim, that Ling should be overruled, and that the supreme court should judicially establish a third-party cause of action broad enough to include the factual situation herein arising as it did in a social situation. The supreme court disagreed. In the absence of legislation, suppliers of alcohol were not liable to the victims of an intoxicated tortfeasor. The decision to impose liability, and under what circumstances, upon the suppliers of alcohol for the torts of their intoxicated patrons or guests was a matter of public policy which the legislature was best equipped to handle. Even an extremely narrow dram shop law failed to pass, and the 2005 Kansas Legislature had two more opportunities to enact dram shop laws. Both measures failed to pass.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

299 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boley


No. 91,804


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 989; 113 P.3d 248; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 363



June 10, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 32 Kan. App. 2d 1192, 95 P.3d 1022 (2004). Appeal from Harvey district court; RICHARD B. WALKER, judge.
State v. Boley, 32 Kan. App. 2d 1192, 95 P.3d 1022, 2004 Kan. App. LEXIS 886 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is vacated and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which remanded the prisoner's case for resentencing and ruled that because resentencing would frustrate the State's purpose in entering the plea agreement, upon remand the State could withdraw from the plea agreement and refile the charges that were dismissed pursuant to that agreement or choose to perform under the plea agreement as modified.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner pled no contest to one count of attempt to manufacture methamphetamine, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 65-4159(a) and 21-3301, a severity level 1 drug felony. The State agreed to recommend a downward durational departure sentence of 48 months' imprisonment. The district court accepted the plea after questioning the prisoner to ascertain the voluntariness of his plea. On appeal, the court rejected the appellate court's determination that the State could withdraw from the plea under the frustration of purpose doctrine. The court noted that the State's purposes were achieved by the plea agreement. Through the agreement, the State avoided a trial and the attendant risk of a not guilty verdict. The court concluded that the elements of the frustration of purpose doctrine were not met under the facts of the case and that therefore the State could not rescind the plea agreement in this case based upon a mistake of law. The State could not, in its discretion, withdraw from its plea agreement with the prisoner on the basis that the case was remanded with directions to resentence the prisoner to a lesser penalty.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is vacated and remanded with directions.

 

300 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dixon


No. 89,164


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 563; 112 P.3d 883; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 335



June 3, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Dixon, 289 Kan. 46, 209 P.3d 675, 2009 Kan. LEXIS 176 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Lyon district court; MERLIN G. WHEELER, Judge.
State v. Griffin, 279 Kan. 634, 112 P.3d 862, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 349 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Lyon District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of two counts of felony murder, five counts of aggravated battery, two counts of burglary, and one count each of theft, criminal damage to property, aggravated assault, and criminal possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions and the supreme court reversed and remanded for a new trial. The supreme court stated that a jury could have found beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant committed aggravated arson in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3719. Thus, aggravated arson could have supported his felony murder and burglary convictions. However, the supreme court also held that it was improper for the prosecutor by questions and comments to draw incriminatory inferences from defendant's constitutional right under the Fourteenth Amendment to employ counsel as an element of the right to a fair trial. The jurors might have decided that defendant was guilty because the prosecutor implied that defendant must have been guilty by repeatedly eliciting testimony and commenting on defendant's contacting counsel. Under such circumstances, the error was not harmless. Further, it was error for the trial court to close the courtroom for announcement of the verdicts. Thus, defendant was denied his right to have a public trial. The court concluded that the evidence sufficiently established defendant's criminal responsibility for the explosion and fire.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed and remanded for a new trial, holding in part that there was prosecutorial misconduct and that defendant was deprived of his right to a public trial. The remainder of the judgment was affirmed.

 

301 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Montoy v. State


No. 92,032


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 817; 112 P.3d 923; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 347



June 3, 2005, Supplemental Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.
Montoy v. State, 102 P.3d 1160, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 2 (Kan., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee citizens sued appellants, the State of Kansas, the board members, and the commissioner, in Shawnee County District Court (Kansas), to challenge the public school finance system. On appeal, the Supreme Court of Kansas ruled that the Kansas Legislature failed to make suitable provision for finance of the public school system. The Legislature subsequently presented its remedy for determination of whether it complied with the order.


OVERVIEW: In a prior order in the case, the state supreme court determined that the Kansas Legislature had failed to make suitable provision for finance of the public school system because the financing formula used to finance the public school system in the State of Kansas did not comply with Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6. The Legislature then passed H.B. 2247 (Kan. 2005) in response to the court's order for remedial action, while the court retained jurisdiction. The court was then required to review the remedial school finance legislation to see if it complied with the court's order by remedying the infirmities in the Kansas School District Finance and Quality Performance Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-6405 et seq., identified in the court's prior opinion, and made suitable provision for financing of education by bringing the State's school financing formula into compliance with Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6. The court concluded that a continuing lack of constitutionally adequate funding together with the inequity-producing local property tax measures meant that the school financing formula, as altered by H.B. 2247, still fell short of the standard set by Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6.


OUTCOME: The Kansas Legislative Division of Post Audit was commissioned to conduct a comprehensive and extensive cost study to be presented to the Kansas Legislature, the court issued financing directives for additional funding, and the court retained jurisdiction of the appeal. If necessary, further action was to be taken by the court as was deemed advisable to ensure compliance with the opinion.

 

302 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re the Appeal of CIG Field Servs. Co.


No. 92,655


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 857; 112 P.3d 138; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 341



June 3, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural gas gathering company sought review of the decision from respondent Kansas Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA), which upheld the Department of Revenue's valuations and assessments for tax years 1997 to 2003 and applied a statutory differentiation under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a01 between the tax treatment for interstate and intercounty gas gathering systems on the one hand and intracounty gas gathering systems on the other.


OVERVIEW: The company's gas gathering system crossed the Kansas state line into Oklahoma and Colorado. The Kansas Department of Revenue valued the company's interstate and intercounty gas gathering systems at 33 percent of its fair market value pursuant to § 79-5a01 and Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-1439(b)(2)(C). In contrast, it valued the company's intracounty gathering system at a lower rate because it was "commercial and industrial" property and not a "public utility" under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a01. The BOTA majority ruled that the cumulative appraised value of the company's property was $11 million higher than it would have been if the company was an intracounty gas gathering system. On appeal, the court extended the U.S. Supreme Court's rationale from the Dean Milk case and determined that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a01 was facially discriminatory and had the effect of unduly burdening interstate commerce. The court found also that the additional $900,000 in taxes that the company had to pay could not be characterized as a de minimis or even minor impact. Accordingly, the court found that § 79-5a01's statutory differentiation was unconstitutional under the federal Commerce Clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded the cause, ruling that Kansas's statutory differentiation between the tax treatment for interstate and intercounty gas gathering systems on the one hand and intracounty gas gathering systems on the other violated the Commerce Clause.

 

303 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Cross v. Kan. Dep't of Revenue


No. 92,051


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 501; 110 P.3d 438; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 161



April 29, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Riley district court, DAVID L. STUTZMAN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged a decision from the Riley District Court (Kansas), which entered judgment in favor of appellee Kansas Department of Revenue in a license suspension case.


OVERVIEW: The driver requested an administrative hearing after receiving a notice of suspension. After the suspension was affirmed, the driver sought review before the district court. She contended that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1020(g) was unconstitutional because it denied her due process of law. After the district court entered judgment in favor of the Department, the driver sought review. In affirming, the court determined that § 8-1020(g) limited the witnesses at the hearing to any officer who signed the certification form and to one other witness that was present at the issuance of the certification. Only one officer was involved in the driver's detention, testing, and arrest. The driver did not proffer the substance of testimony that she expected from other witnesses that she wanted to call. In light of the officer's uncontradicted testimony, the driver's own admissions concerning the consumption of alcohol, and her mere speculation about the testimony of other witnesses, she did not establish that any other relevant witnesses existed. As such, § 8-1020(g), as applied to the driver, did not deny her due process of law. She also had no standing to mount a facial constitutional attack.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

304 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. White


No. 90,661


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 326; 109 P.3d 1199; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 156



April 22, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. White, 2007 Kan. LEXIS 363 (Kan., June 22, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the District Court of Butler County (Kansas) that convicted him of first-degree premeditated murder and imposed a hard 25 life sentence. Defendant claimed the trial court erred in excluding his expert's testimony regarding his lack of mental state or mental capacity to commit the murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder after he drove two hours to a store where his son-in-law worked, walked in, and shot him three times. Defendant filed notice of his intent to rely on the defense of lack of mental state or mental capacity as provided Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3220 based on his expert's psychological evaluation that showed defendant was suffering from depression. Defendant's guardianship of his grandson had just been terminated, and defendant suspected his son-in-law was sexually abusing the child. The trial court granted the State's motion to exclude the expert's testimony. On appeal, the court held that the trial court violated defendant's due process right to present his defense by refusing to allow his expert to testify, by failing to instruct the jury on the defense of mental disease or defect, and by instructing the jury that mental disease or defect was not a defense. The court held that § 22-3220 removed insanity and diminished capacity as separate defenses and instead focused on a lack of the mental state required as an element of an offense; therefore, the expert should have been allowed to testify about defendant's lack of intent to commit the murder.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment and remanded for a new trial.

State v. James


No. 90,756


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 354; 109 P.3d 1171; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 148



April 22, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at James v. State, 293 P.3d 815, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 102 (Kan. Ct. App., 2013)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at James v. State, 344 P.3d 970, 2015 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 195 (Kan. Ct. App., 2015)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied James v. Heimgartner, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45819 (D. Kan., Mar. 28, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; JOHN J. McNALLY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Wyandotte County District Court, Kansas, convicting him of two counts of first-degree premeditated murder, and sentencing him to concurrent hard 50 life sentences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant testified at his trial and denied shooting either victim. Defendant argued that exhibits 94 and 95 should have been excluded from evidence. The State argued that the photographs were generally relevant for the purpose of establishing the extent and nature of the first victim's wounds, and emphasized that the pathologist referred to the two pictures while explaining the cause of death, which was helpful to the jury's understanding of his medical testimony. The State also claimed the pictures were probative on the manner of death and the violent nature of the crimes. The appellate court ruled that the State's exhibits 94 and 95 showed the extent, nature, and number of wounds inflicted, and assisted the jury's understanding of medical testimony. The exhibits helped the pathologist explain the cause of death and proved the manner of death and the violent nature of the crime. The photographs were unpleasant but not gruesome. The photographs were not repetitious or cumulative, but rather the only two photographs showing the victim from particular angles. Also, the Kansas hard 50 sentencing statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4638 (Supp. 2004), was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

306 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Robertson


No. 90,319


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 291; 109 P.3d 1174; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 150



April 22, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Robertson v. State, 152 P.3d 110, 2007 Kan. App. LEXIS 177 (Kan. Ct. App., Feb. 23, 2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Robertson, 298 Kan. 342, 312 P.3d 361, 2013 Kan. LEXIS 1243 (Nov. 8, 2013)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Robertson, 2017 Kan. Unpub. LEXIS 1 (Kan., May 12, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Butler district court; MIKE E. WARD, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Butler District Court (Kansas) of first-degree murder, arson, and aggravated burglary. He received a hard 50 life sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that he did not voluntarily waive his Miranda rights. The supreme court disagreed. Before the investigator asked defendant a single question about the crime, defendant was blaming the victim's daughter and questioning her motive. Indeed, every time defendant mentioned an attorney, he spontaneously reinitiated conversation with the officers about the crimes. Specifically, after the investigator was finally able to deliver the Miranda warnings, defendant disclosed how he and the daughter set the fire. That disclosure was not prompted by questioning. The investigator then asked defendant if he wanted to continue talking about the case. Defendant responded that he had signed the Miranda form and would speak with the investigator but might not answer all of his questions. He also advised the investigator that he had been arrested before and therefore understood his Miranda rights and his ability to waive them. Under the totality of the circumstances, the facts found by the trial court were supported by substantial competent evidence, and defendant's motion to suppress his statement was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Buehler-May


No. 91,470


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 371; 110 P.3d 425; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 149



April 22, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Buehler-May v. Kansas, 546 U.S. 980, 126 S. Ct. 549, 163 L. Ed. 2d 465, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 7913 (Oct. 31, 2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at Buehler-May v. State, 321 P.3d 36, 2014 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 199 (Kan. Ct. App., Mar. 28, 2014)
Post-conviction relief denied at Buehler-May v. State, 2017 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 619 (Kan. Ct. App., July 28, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; THOMAS L. BOEDING, judge.
State v. Cavaness, 278 Kan. 469, 101 P.3d 717, 2004 Kan. LEXIS 732 (Dec. 3, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) found defendant guilty of premeditated first-degree murder, conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, and aggravated kidnapping. The trial court imposed a hard 50 life sentence for the murder conviction, 155 months' imprisonment for the aggravated kidnapping conviction, and 117 months' imprisonment for the conspiracy conviction, all sentences to run concurrently. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant did not establish error in the trial court's decision to deny his motion to endorse the psychiatrist's testimony because (1) the motion to endorse, if considered a notice of the defense of mental disease or defect under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3219, was untimely; (2) defendant did not establish good cause for the delayed notice as there was some question as to his mental status well before his July 2003 trial setting and there was more than sufficient time for any necessary mental examinations; (3) the trial court did not err in failing to consider the factors used to determine whether to exclude the testimony of a defense witness because no notice of a defense under § 22-3219 had been given; and (4) the notice requirements of § 22-3219 did not deprive defendant of his constitutional due process right to defend against the State's case or the constitutional right to a fair trial. Also, there was sufficient evidence to support the conclusion that defendant committed the murder in an especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel manner. Thus, the trial court's use of that aggravating factor in determining defendant's sentence was not erroneous.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

308 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Trees Oil Co. v. State Corp. Comm'n


No. 91,733


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 209; 105 P.3d 1269; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 57; 162 Oil & Gas Rep. 462



February 18, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Haskell district court, TOM R. SMITH, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an unwilling minority participant in an oil and gas unit, sought review of a decision from the District Court of Haskell County (Kansas), which affirmed an administrative order that granted the application of appellee operating company to allow compulsory unitization and unit operation pursuant to the Kansas Unitization Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1301 et seq., over the continuing objection of the minority participant.


OVERVIEW: The operating company wished to inject water into a formation channel to increase oil production. The minority participant objected to the unitization application. The evidence showed that the natural state of the formations had been altered by commingling formations in some of the well bores. The parties' wells were in pressure communication, which meant that a change in pressure in one area would affect other areas. The court held that the proposed unitization met the statutory requirements for unit operation of a pool. As defined in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1302, a pool was characterized by a single pressure system so that production from one part of the pool affected the reservoir pressure throughout its extent. An amendment to § 55-1302 did not alter this definition. The agency's finding that the unit operating agreement was fair and equitable under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1304 (2003 Supp.) was supported by substantial competent evidence, pursuant to the standards set forth in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-621(c)(7), (8). The use of prior data in evaluating production was not arbitrary or capricious. Exclusion of the minority participant's acreage would violate correlative rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision in favor of the operating company.

 

309 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jones


No. 89,658


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 71; 106 P.3d 1; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 142



February 18, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed Appeal from Ellis district court; THOMAS L. TOEPFER, judge.
State v. Jones, 78 P.3d 497, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 940 (Kan. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is reversed. Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Kansas Court of Appeals, which held that he had consented to a preliminary breath test (PBT), and which affirmed a trial court's judgment convicting him of driving or attempting to drive a vehicle while having a blood alcohol level greater than .08 in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567(a)(2). The trial court had denied defendant's motion to suppress the alleged involuntary PBT and the resultant blood test.


OVERVIEW: At issue on appeal was whether defendant gave voluntary, knowing, and intelligent consent to the search of his deep lung air through the use of a PBT. The court held that taking defendant's deep lung air was a search requiring, under the circumstances, his consent. The particular PBT used on defendant tested his deep lung breath for chemical analysis and, thus, was a search subject to the strictures of the Fourth Amendment. The State did not meet its burdens of establishing that defendant's consent to the search was voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently given or that his consent was impliedly given. Defendant's mere acquiescence to the PBT did not establish voluntary consent. If an officer established only the minimum of one of the predicates for testing listed in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1012, he could not request that a driver be tested via the implied consent provisions contained in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1001(a) because application of the implied consent provisions required meeting two predicates in § 8-1001(b). The results of the PBT were not admissible, and the subsequent blood test results were also inadmissible. Without that evidence, defendant's conviction could not stand.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the appellate court and the trial court.

 

310 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Martens


No. 91,827


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 242; 106 P.3d 28; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 59



February 18, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court, LARRY McCLAIN, judge.



DISPOSITION: District court judgment affirmed in part; remaining issues dismissed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee wife filed an action under the Civil Protection from Stalking Act (Act), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-31a01 et seq. (Supp. 2003), seeking a protective order against appellant former husband. The Johnson District Court, Kansas, ruled that the Act was constitutional, issued a 1-year protection from stalking order (PSO), and awarded the wife $ 5,000 in attorney fees. The husband appealed.


OVERVIEW: The husband challenged the constitutionality of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-31a02 (Supp. 2003) as both unconstitutionally vague on its face and overbroad. While the PSO that had been entered against the husband had expired by the time the case was heard on appeal, the state supreme court agreed to address the issue as the constitutionality of the Act was a matter of public importance capable of repetition. The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's determination that § 63-31a02 was constitutional. The state supreme court held that the statute was not vague, as two objective standards were used to modify the terms "alarms," "annoys," "torments," or "terrorizes," and the statute clearly conveyed a sufficiently definite warning as to the proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practice. Also, the term "legitimate purpose" was subject to an objective standard and excluded constitutionally protected conduct. The statute was not overbroad, as the inclusion of objective standards and the express statement to exclude constitutionally protected speech indicated it was sufficiently tailored so as not infringe on First Amendment rights.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's decision regarding the constitutionality of the statute and dismissed all other issues on appeal as moot.

State v. Campbell


Nos. 88,654, 88,656


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


279 Kan. 1; 106 P.3d 1129; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 9



January 31, 2005, Modified Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in State v. Campbell, 31 Kan. App. 2d 1123, 78 P.3d 1178 (2003). Appeal from Reno district court; RICHARD J. ROME, judge. Original opinion, State v. Campbell, 278 Kan. 410, 101 P.3d 1179, filed December 3, 2004, is ordered withdrawn from publication.
State v. Campbell, 278 Kan. 410, 101 P.3d 1179, 2004 Kan. LEXIS 723 (Kan., 2004)
State v. Campbell, 31 Kan. App. 2d 1123, 78 P.3d 1178, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 956 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court on the limited issue subject to our grant of review is reversed. Judgment of the district court on that issue is reversed, the sentence is vacated, and the case is remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Kansas Court of Appeals, which affirmed a district court's decision regarding sentencing for possession of ephedrine with intent to manufacture methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was sentenced for violating Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-7006, he filed an appeal. He argued that he should have been sentenced under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4152 instead. Defendant relied on the Frazier decision to support his argument. However, the court of appeals found that Frazier was wrongly decided, and affirmed the sentence. In reversing, the court determined that the court of appeals' decision regarding Frazier was incorrect. The rule was that where two criminal offenses had identical elements, but were classified differently for purposes of imposing a penalty, a defendant convicted of either crime could have only been sentenced under the lesser penalty provision. The court noted that there were three situations where defendant's conduct was covered by two statutes. The court sought to curb the situation where unfettered prosecutorial discretion was permitted. The statutes in question were overlapping rather than duplicative. Because the elements under Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 65-4152, 65-7006 were the same whether defendant was charged under either statute, he should have been sentenced under the lesser penalty provisions of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4152(a)(3).


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals and the district court on the issue of sentencing was reversed. The sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded with directions to resentence defendant under the statute with the lesser penalty.

 

312 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Montoy v. State


No. 92,032


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


102 P.3d 1160; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 2



January 3, 2005, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Judgment entered by Montoy v. State, 279 Kan. 817, 112 P.3d 923, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 347 (2005)
Adhered to, Stay lifted by, Dismissed by, Remanded by Montoy v. State, 138 P.3d 755, 2006 Kan. LEXIS 479 (Kan., July 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.
Montoy v. State, 275 Kan. 145, 62 P.3d 228, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 16 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case challenging the public education funding scheme, the Shawnee District Court, Kansas, held that the Kansas School District Finance and Quality Performance Act (SDFQPA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-6405 et seq., was unconstitutional. Defendants, the State and individuals with the board of education, appealed. Plaintiffs, school districts and students, cross appealed a finding that the legislature did not abrogate the board's obligations.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the finding that SDFQPA did not violate the Kansas or U.S. Equal Protection Clauses because the funding differentials as provided were rationally related to a legitimate legislative purpose. SDFQPA was also not unconstitutional based solely on its "disparate impact." The court found, however, that substantial evidence supported a holding that the legislature failed to meet its burden under Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6 to "make suitable provision for finance" of the public schools. Based on the record and the legislature's own definition of "suitable education," Kan. Stat. Ann. § 46-1225(e) (Supp. 2003), the standard was not being met under the current financing formula. Specifically, SDFQPA failed to provide adequate funding for a suitable education for students of middle- and large-sized districts with a high proportion of minority and/or at-risk and special education students. Some school districts were forced to use local option budgets to finance general education, rather than "extra" expenses as intended. Failure to do any cost analysis distorted the low enrollment, special education, vocational, bilingual education, and the at-risk student weighting factors.


OUTCOME: In a brief decision, the court reversed the finding of equal protection violations, affirmed as to failure to make suitable provision for public finance, and affirmed on the cross appeal. The court retained jurisdiction to allow the legislature to correct the infirmity, with the present formula in effect until further order. The court withheld formal opinion pending corrective legislation or until April 12, 2005 and stayed issuance of mandate.

 

313 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Montoy v. State


No. 92,032


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 769; 120 P.3d 306; 2005 Kan. LEXIS 460



January 3, 2005, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.
Montoy v. State, 275 Kan. 145, 62 P.3d 228, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 16 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a suit brought by appellee school districts, appellants, the State and the state board of education, appealed from a decision of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas) holding that the Kansas School District Finance and Quality Performance Act (SDFQPA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-6405 et seq., was unconstitutional. The district court made findings regarding the various statutory and societal changes which occurred after the decision in U.S.D. No. 229.


OVERVIEW: The issue was the constitutionality of the statutory scheme for funding the public schools. Inter alia, the Supreme Court of Kansas held that the SDFQPA financing formula did not have an unconstitutional disparate impact on minorities and/or other classes. However, the legislature failed to meet its burden as imposed by Kan. Const. art. 6, § 6 to "make suitable provision for finance" of the public schools. The court needed to look no further than the legislature's own definition of suitable education, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 46-1225(e) (2003), to determine that the standard was not being met under the current financing formula. In particular, the school districts (Salina and Dodge City) established that the SDFQPA failed to provide adequate funding for a suitable education for students of their and other similarly situated districts, i.e., middle- and large-sized districts with a high proportion of minority and/or at-risk and special education students. Also, while the original intent of the provision for local option budgets within the financing formula was to fund "extra" expenses, some school districts had been forced to use local option budgets to finance general education.


OUTCOME: Inter alia, the district court's holding that the SDFQPA's financing formula was a violation of equal protection was reversed. The district court's holding that the SDFQPA financing formula has an unconstitutional disparate impact on minorities and/or other classes was reversed. The district court's holding that the legislature had failed to meet its burden to make suitable provision for finance of the public schools was affirmed.

 

314 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hurt


No. 90,571


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 676; 101 P.3d 1249; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 773



December 17, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Hurt v. State, 2008 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 304 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) convicted defendant of first-degree premeditated murder and aggravated assault arising out of the shooting death of his former girlfriend. The trial court sentenced him to a hard 50 life sentence for the first-degree murder conviction. Defendant appealed his convictions and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's argument of prosecutorial misconduct, based on the concept of temporal concurrence, or the idea that the intent must exist at the same time as the act, was technically correct. However, his interpretation of the prosecutor's statement was too broad. The prosecutor's statements were fair comment on the evidence. That defendant was convicted of premeditated first-degree murder showed that any error in the prosecutor's statement was harmless. Defendant's challenge to the hard 50 sentencing scheme was based on the holding of Apprendi, that facts increasing the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum had to be proved to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. Exposing a defendant to a later parole eligibility was not the equivalent of exposing him to an increased maximum penalty. Defendant contended that, because the State failed to show the victim was in reasonable apprehension of immediate bodily harm, his conviction for aggravated assault had to be reversed. A rational factfinder could easily find that defendant placed him in reasonable apprehension of immediate bodily harm.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Denney


No. 90,454


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 643; 101 P.3d 1257; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 778



December 17, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Denney v. Roberts, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 43815 (D. Kan., June 23, 2006)
Appeal after remand at, Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Denney, 283 Kan. 781, 156 P.3d 1275, 2007 Kan. LEXIS 246 (2007)
Related proceeding at Denney v. Werholtz, 348 Fed. Appx. 348, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 21529 (10th Cir. Kan., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.
State v. Denney, 26 P.3d 729, 2001 Kan. App. LEXIS 637 (Kan. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Sedgwick district court (Kansas) denied defendant's motions for correction of an illegal sentence and for Deoxyribonucleic Acid (DNA) testing pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2512 (2003 Supp.). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape and aggravated burglary. While he was on parole he committed several offenses, including aggravated criminal sodomy. Defendant was convicted of aggravated criminal sodomy in two cases. His convictions were affirmed, but the appellate court held that defendant was entitled to have his prior sentences converted. On remand, the trial court reduced defendant's sentences in the original case. Defendant filed a motion to correct an illegal sentence, arguing that the sentence from the first sodomy case should also have been converted. The motion was denied. The court affirmed, stating that defendant was not on parole in the first sodomy case when he committed the acts in second sodomy case. However, the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion for DNA testing. Although the court held that § 21-2512 was unconstitutional because it failed to include defendant's specific situation, the court concluded that § 21-2512 should not be nullified but rather extended to allow a defendant convicted of aggravated criminal sodomy for penetrating a female's anus with his penis to petition for DNA testing. Therefore, the court remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to correct an illegal sentence but that it did err in denying his motion for DNA testing. Therefore, the court remanded to the trial court for further determination of whether defendant met the remaining qualifications for DNA testing under the applicable statute.

 

316 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re Appeal of Sprint Communs. Co., L.P.


Nos. 90,663, 90,664, 90,665, 90,666, 90,667


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 690; 101 P.3d 1239; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 776



December 17, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA) upheld the Department of Revenue's (Department) denial of refund claims sought by appellant taxpayers (telecommunication companies). The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether equipment used to provide telecommunications services was machinery and equipment utilized to manufacture tangible personal property for resale or to provide a service. The taxpayers argued their machinery and equipment qualified for exemption under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3606(kk) because it was comprised entirely of tangible personal property (electricity). The Kansas Retailers' Sales Tax Act defined telecommunications as a service, not tangible personal property. Equipment utilized in engineering a telecommunication product or in the process of controlling or measuring the telecommunication process was not exempt from sales taxation under the exemption provision of § 79-3606(kk), which applied to purchases of machinery and equipment used to manufacture tangible personal property for resale. Requiring telecommunications companies to pay sales tax on purchases of equipment utilized in engineering a telecommunication product or in the process of controlling or measuring the telecommunication process and determining that the exemption did not apply to such purchases did not violate the taxpayers' equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
