State v. Marsh


No. 81,135


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 520; 102 P.3d 445; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 772



December 17, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by Kansas v. Marsh, 544 U.S. 1060, 125 S. Ct. 2517, 161 L. Ed. 2d 1109, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 4364 (2005)
Reversed by, Remanded by Kansas v. Marsh, 126 S. Ct. 2516, 165 L. Ed. 2d 429, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5163 (U.S., 2006)
Vacated by, in part, Remanded by State v. Marsh, 2006 Kan. LEXIS 841 (Kan., Oct. 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court, CLARK V. OWENS II, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of capital murder, first-degree premeditated murder, aggravated arson, and aggravated burglary. He was sentenced to death for the capital offense, life imprisonment with a mandatory minimum term of 40 years for the other murder, 51 months for aggravated arson, and 34 months for aggravated burglary. The last three sentences were to be served consecutively.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support his convictions and that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4624(e), which provided that in doubtful cases the jury had to return a sentence of death, was unconstitutional on its face. The court found that the evidence was sufficient to establish that defendant set fire to the victims' house, that one of the victim's burns was the proximate cause of her death, and that defendant acted intentionally and with premeditation in killing the other victim. However, the trial court committed reversible error when it failed to engage in an evaluation of the totality of facts and circumstances under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-407(f)'s relevance standard to determine whether defendant's third-party evidence of the connection of the capital murder victim's husband to the crime was admissible. The evidence was sufficient to support a finding that aggravating circumstances in support of imposition of a hard 40 sentence were not outweighed by mitigating circumstances. The court concluded that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4624(e) was unconstitutional on its face, which rendered moot guilt and penalty phase issues dependent on imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences for aggravated burglary and first-degree premeditated murder. The court reversed defendant's convictions for capital murder and aggravated arson and remanded for a new trial.

State v. Drennan


No. 90,954


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 704; 101 P.3d 1218; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 774



December 17, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Drennan v. State, 240 P.3d 986, 2010 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 797 (Kan. Ct. App., 2010)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Drennan v. State, 314 P.3d 900, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1117 (Kan. Ct. App., 2013)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied, Motion denied by Drennan v. Pryor, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 49898 (D. Kan., Apr. 13, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; TERRY L. PULLMAN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) that convicted him of first-degree murder of his girlfriend by strangulation and sentenced him to a hard 50 life sentence. Defendant claimed the trial court erred in not giving instructions on lesser included offenses; in admitting evidence of prior bad acts; in denying his motion to suppress; in denying Batson challenges; and in imposing a hard 50 sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction and sentence. The court held that defendant failed to preserve the issue of a lesser included offense instruction on voluntary manslaughter and that there was no evidence to support the element of severe provocation or of recklessness for reckless second-degree murder and involuntary manslaughter. The victim was found with a cord around her neck and injuries indicating that she was intentionally choked for 4-6 minutes, which caused brain death. The court rejected defendant's claim that he was incapable of forming the requisite intent due to his intoxication, and held that defendant placed his intent at issue, therefore evidence of attacks on a prior girlfriend were properly admitted to show his intent and premeditation in the murder. The court held that the motion to suppress was properly denied because the police officer's entry into his home was justified by the emergency doctrine exception to the warrant requirement. The officers knew the victim lived there, and a neighbor told them he heard an argument and a woman's scream. The court rejected defendant's claims of error as to the findings as to aggravating and mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment.

State v. Campbell


Nos. 88,654, 88,656


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 410; 101 P.3d 1179; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 723



December 3, 2004, Opinion Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] Kan. LEXIS - 1THIS OPINION WAS WITHDRAWN BY THE COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by State v. Campbell, 279 Kan. 1, 106 P.3d 1129, 2005 Kan. LEXIS 9 (Kan., Jan. 31, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in State v. Campbell, 31 Kan. App. 2d 1123, 78 P.3d 1178 (2003). Appeal from Reno district court; RICHARD J. ROME, judge.
State v. Campbell, 31 Kan. App. 2d 1123, 78 P.3d 1178, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 956 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Kansas Court of Appeals, which affirmed a district court's decision to convict him of six drugs offenses. Specifically, defendant challenged the sentences imposed for possession of ephedrine with intent to manufacture methamphetamine and possession of drug paraphernalia with intent to use it to manufacture a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with various offenses after drugs and other items were found inside a residence. After defendant was convicted, he was sentenced under the unlawful possession of ephedrine with intent to manufacture methamphetamine statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-7006(d) (Supp. 2000). Defendant contended that he should have been sentenced under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4152 (Supp. 2000), the statute for possession of drug paraphernalia. After the sentence was affirmed, defendant again sought review. In affirming, the court determined that a sentence under the statute carrying the lesser penalty was required if the two offenses were identical. The court found that the statutes at issue were overlapping rather than duplicative. As such, the court had to determine if they gave the prosecutor unfettered sentencing discretion. The drug paraphernalia charge included such items as jars, dishes, microwave, lithium batteries, filters, funnels, plastic lids, and baggies. The charge did not include "products or materials" within the meaning of Kan. Stat. Ann. 65-4150(c); therefore, it was not identical to the charge of possession of ephedrine with the intent to manufacture methamphetamine.


OUTCOME: The sentences were affirmed.

State v. Ramirez


No. 89,446


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 402; 100 P.3d 94; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 656



November 12, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed Appeal from Finney district court; THOMAS F. RICHARDSON, judge.
State v. Ramirez, 81 P.3d 1275, 2004 Kan. App. LEXIS 15 (Kan. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirming the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which affirmed the trial court judgment that denied her motion to suppress and convicted and sentenced her for possession of cocaine and possession of marijuana. The trial court and court of appeals held that, under the totality of the circumstances, the officer had probable cause to arrest defendant and that a warrantless search could be upheld as a search incident to arrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the search that revealed the cocaine was unconstitutional. The evidence showed that defendant was arrested in a bar known for drug activity after a law enforcement officer, who had known her for 8 years and knew she used drugs in the past, observed that she was holding a piece of a torn plastic baggie in her hand, was under the influence, and was uncharacteristically avoiding eye contact with the officer and acting nervous. The officer grabbed her wrist and opened her hand, which revealed the cocaine. The State did not contest the trial court's finding that the officer arrested defendant when he grabbed her wrist. The court held that a combination of several factors created probable cause, because the facts and circumstances within the law enforcement officer's knowledge were sufficient to warrant a person of reasonable caution in the belief that the offense of possession was being committed. The court held that, because there was probable cause to arrest defendant, her arrest did not contravene the Fourth and Fourteenth Amendments or Kan. Const. Bill Rights § 15 and the search, as a search incident to arrest was lawful.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals judgment.

Rice v. State

SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 309; 95 P.3d 994; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 454

322 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Messer


No. 90,007


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 161; 91 P.3d 1191; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 407



June 25, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; PETER V. RUDDICK, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Johnson District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of misdemeanor false impersonation of a Kansas Bureau of Investigation (KBI) agent in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3824.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's neighbor reported to authorities that defendant brought up the neighbor's ex-wife and children, saying in part that he knew where the ex-wife lived, where she worked, the places she frequented, and with whom she associated. When the neighbor asked how defendant knew those things, defendant responded that he was a "triple agent" with the KBI. He was subsequently convicted of misdemeanor false impersonation of a KBI agent and the supreme court affirmed on appeal. Acceptance of defendant's overbreadth argument required the supreme court to reweigh defendant's and the neighbor's credibility, which was not its function. The district judge who heard and observed both men's testimony found the neighbor's version of the conversation more believable and the supreme court could not reject the neighbor's version of the conversation and adopt defendant's on appeal. Further, defendant's statement was not mere puffing and did not test the outer limits of constitutionally protected speech. Because defendant's sufficiency claim relied upon elements of the crime that the supreme court refused to read into the legislature's choice of statutory language, his claim failed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

323 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Huff


Nos. 90,850, 90,854


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 214; 92 P.3d 604; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 410



June 25, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Saline district court; DANIEL L. HEBERT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Saline District Court (Kansas), which granted defendants' motion to suppress the evidence found by police in a search. The State also appealed the trial court's subsequent dismissal of the drug charges against defendants.


OVERVIEW: The State appealed the trial court's grant of defendants' suppression motion and the dismissal of the drug charges against defendants. The court held that the trial court's decision to dismiss and its decision to grant defendants' suppression motions were one and the same; thus, the State's citation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3602(b)(1) (Supp. 2003), which authorized an appeal from the dismissals, was sufficient to preserve the State's right to challenge the basis of the dismissal decisions. The warrantless entry by police officers into an apartment where defendants were social guests was not excusable when a warrantless search of the residence would be unconstitutional. There were not exigent circumstances that allowed the entry of the police into the apartment, because loud music and the smell of marijuana did not indicate that any suspect was likely to be charged with a violent offense. Application of the inevitable discovery doctrine was inappropriate, because it was not inevitable that defendants would have remained at the apartment, even if the officers had stood guard outside.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

324 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Barnes


No. 89,628


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


278 Kan. 121; 92 P.3d 578; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 421



June 25, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed. Appeal from Washington district court; J. STEPHEN NYSWONGER and THOMAS M. TUGGLE, judges.
State v. Barnes, 78 P.3d 498, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 967 (Kan. Ct. App., 2003).



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' judgment reversed. District court judgment reversed. Sentence vacated and case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to aiding and abetting the manufacture of methamphetamine, possession of methamphetamine, possession of methamphetamine with intent to sell, possession of drug paraphernalia, and endangering a child. Defendant appealed, and the Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended that her guilty plea was of no significance because the imposition of a drug severity level 1 felony sentence was an illegal sentence. The supreme court found that the trial court had jurisdiction to find defendant guilty and impose sentence. Further, defendant's sentence conformed to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4159 as to both its character and term of punishment. Defendant's sentence was not ambiguous in the time or manner in which it was to be served. Thus, defendant's sentence was not "illegal." However, the supreme court also found that Sub. H.B. 2777 (codified at 23-21 Kan. Reg. 720 (2004)) increased the penalty to which defendant would have been subject if the legislation was applied to her. Without the legislation, she would have been sentenced for a drug severity level 3 felony; if the legislation was effective as to her, she would have been sentenced for a drug severity level 1 felony. Therefore, the retroactivity provision of the house bill could not have been constitutionally applied to defendant so as to avoid an ex post facto violation.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was vacated and the case remanded for resentencing.

Doe v. State Dep't of Human Res.


No. 90,124


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 795; 90 P.3d 940; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 262



May 14, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court, TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.
Acosta v. Nat'l Beef Packing Co., 273 Kan. 385, 44 P.3d 330, 2002 Kan. LEXIS 144 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Shawnee District Court (Kansas) affirmed a final order of appellee Kansas Department of Human Resources imposing a civil fine against appellant workers' compensation claimant for fraudulent or abusive acts under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-5,120 (1993). The claimant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant was an illegal immigrant who obtained employment using an assumed name and social security number. The claimant was injured during the course of her employment and filed a workers' compensation action using the assumed name. The district court determined that the use of an assumed name constituted a fraudulent or abusive act under § 44-5,120(d)(4)(A) or (B). On appeal, the court affirmed, concluding that the claimant's use of an assumed name in applying for benefits and lying under oath were abusive, notwithstanding the claimant's legal entitlement to the benefits. The court found that the concealment of the employee's true identity amounted to the concealment of a material fact under § 44-5,120(d)(4)(B). The court stated that, without knowledge of the claimant's true identity, her employer would not be able to obtain and verify her previous medical or work history. The court also rejected the claimant's argument that the order was based upon alienage and therefore unconstitutional. The court stated that the claimant was not being penalized based on her alien status, but for her actions in intentionally and willfully using a false identity throughout the proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

 

326 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Van Hoet


No. 90,132


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 815; 89 P.3d 606; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 269



May 14, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court, STEPHEN R. TATUM, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to domestic battery and assault. After a competency evaluation, she was diagnosed with schizoaffective disorder, bipolar type, among other things, and the State stipulated that she was insane at the time of the offense. The Johnson district court (Kansas) found her not guilty by lack of mental state, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3428 (Supp. 2003), and committed her to a state hospital for a 90-day evaluation. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that § 22-3428(1)(a) granted her a right to an immediate hearing after her acquittal by reason of mental disease or defect as to whether she was a harm to herself or others. The court disagreed, finding that the trial court's order was consistent with the express provisions of the statute. Language in the second sentence regarding prima facie evidence, when considered with the entire statute, justified language in the first sentence mandating commitment. That subsection contained no express provision for a hearing, but § 22-3428(1)(b), (c), and (d) set forth a comprehensive procedure, including a full hearing within 120 days. The automatic commitment also did not violate due process principles. The limited commitment was simply to conduct an evaluation in order to provide defendant with a precommitment due process hearing. The procedure struck a reasonable balance between the citizens' right to be protected from a defendant acquitted by reason of mental disease and a defendant's right to be free when she was no longer a danger. The procedures and system set forth in the statute were constitutional and did not result in cruel and unusual punishment as applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of commitment.

 

327 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gleason


No. 89,793


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 624; 88 P.3d 218; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 235



April 23, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Gleason v. State, 2007 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 168 (Kan. Ct. App., Aug. 10, 2007)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Motion denied by, Stay denied by Gleason v. McKune, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 127071 (D. Kan., Dec. 1, 2010)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion granted by, Certificate of appealability denied Gleason v. McKune, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 99903 (D. Kan., July 19, 2012)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Gleason v. State, 2015 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 513 (Kan. Ct. App., June 19, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Jefferson district court; GARY L. NAFZIGER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Jefferson District Court (Kansas), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of first-degree felony murder and sentenced defendant to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: The victim was killed with a shotgun during a burglary. Defendant claimed that he was not present, although the evidence showed that he had purchased the shotgun and that his car was used during the crime. The trial court gave an aiding and abetting instruction. The court found no error because the evidence was sufficient for the jury to find defendant guilty as a principal or as an aider and abettor. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3205 did not require that an aider and abettor be physically present during the crime. Likewise, there was no presence requirement in the felony-murder statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401(b). Because burglary was an inherently dangerous felony under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3436, the prosecution did not need to show that the murder was reasonably foreseeable. A prosecutorial violation of a motion in limine was harmless error because the evidence of guilt was overwhelming. Defense counsel was not ineffective with respect to preparation for trial, examining witnesses, objecting to evidence, or an alleged conflict of interest. Because the co-defendants pled guilty to lesser offenses, the trial court was not required to discuss the sentencing disparity on the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence.

State v. Mays


No. 87,519


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 359; 85 P.3d 1208; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 141



March 19, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; THOMAS L. BOEDING and DAVID W. BOAL, judges.
State v. White, 275 Kan. 580, 67 P.3d 138, 2003 Kan. LEXIS 224 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Wyandotte District Court, Kansas, convicting and sentencing him for two counts of first-degree murder, two counts of attempted first-degree murder, two counts of criminal possession of a firearm by a juvenile, and one count of conspiracy to commit first-degree murder. He was sentenced to a controlling term of two consecutive life sentences.


OVERVIEW: This case involved two separate drive-by shootings. The police investigation led to the arrest of several suspects, including defendant, who was then 16 years old. He was properly tried as an adult pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 38-1636(a)(2)(A)(ii), because the crimes would constitute person felonies if committed by an adult. His trial as an adult was not unconstitutional. The 3-month delay between defendant's arrest and preliminary hearing did not violate his right to a speedy trial; because he failed to file a motion to dismiss, he waived the issue. Defendant was not entitled to suppress his statement to police, because he waived his Miranda rights. A doctor was permitted to testify regarding the proximate cause of the victim's death. The jury was properly instructed as to the proximate cause defense. A codefendants' redacted statements were admissible at trial. Based upon the evidence, a rational jury could have found that defendant agreed with his codefendants to kill the victims.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

329 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Kline v. Unified Bd. of Comm'rs


No. 90,578


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 516; 85 P.3d 1237; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 143



March 19, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; JOHN J. BUKATY, JR., judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State brought a quo warranto and declaratory judgment action in the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) against appellee Cities. The State sought an order declaring that charter ordinances that exempted the Cities from the Sunday liquor sales prohibition of Kan Stat. Ann. § 41-712 were invalid and void. The district court held that the Cities had authority to exempt the Cities from the Sunday liquor sales prohibition. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court concluded that, because provisions of the Kansas Liquor Control Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-101 et seq., were not uniformly applicable, the Cities had authority under the Home Rule Amendment to Kan. Const. art. 12, § 5 to exempt the Cities from the Sunday liquor sales prohibition. On appeal, the court affirmed the district court's judgment. The court held that the provision in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-208 prohibiting the cities from passing ordinances in conflict with the Liquor Control Act did not prevent the Cities from exercising home rule with regard to liquor control. As between the will of the people expressed in Kan. Const. art. 12, § 5 and that expressed in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-208, the former prevailed. The court stated that, since the adoption of Kan. Const. art. 12, § 5, the State could preempt a City's authority only by making the provisions of the Liquor Control Act uniformly applicable to all Cities. The court noted that the decision to preempt liquor control still rested with the legislature and that it could decide to do so by making the Liquor Control Act uniformly applicable to all Cities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

330 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davis


No. 87,192


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 309; 85 P.3d 1164; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 133



March 19, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Davis, 302 P.3d 44, 2013 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 492 (Kan. Ct. App., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished opinion filed September 19, 2003. Appeal from Sedgwick district court, JOSEPH BRIBIESCA and REBECCA L. PILSHAW, judges.
State v. Davis, 77 P.3d 1008, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 814 (Kan. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of aggravated kidnapping and attempted rape. On appeal, the Kansas Court of Appeals remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of his allegations regarding his claim of inadequacy of representation; the trial judge rejected defendant's claims. Ultimately, the Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence of 554 months of imprisonment. Defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant raised several issues in arguing that his counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The supreme court agreed, concluding that defendant was substantially prejudiced by defense counsel's inadequacy of representation in failing to seek a pretrial competency evaluation and failing to understand and apply the correct law regarding the defense of mental disease or defect. It was clear that defendant's counsel should have sought another competency hearing prior to trial, considering that defendant had an extensive history of mental illness and frequent commitments and a doctor testified that it was unlikely that defendant could have remained competent throughout the stress of a trial. Further, defendant's counsel did not adequately prepare for trial, because, by his own admission, he was unaware of the proper legal standard for a defense of mental disease or defect. In this regard, his performance was deficient. Applying the Barker factors, the supreme court held that the 15-month delay between the time of defendant's arrest and the date of trial did not violate his constitutional right to a speedy trial.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

In re City of Wichita


No. 89,934


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 487; 86 P.3d 513; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 142



March 19, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.
In re City of Wichita, 274 Kan. 915, 59 P.3d 336, 2002 Kan. LEXIS 787 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City sought review of a decision of the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA), which affirmed the state revenue department's assessment of sales tax, compensating use tax, and interest for transactions by the water utility department. The City also appealed BOTA's denial of attorney fees and the issuance of a protective order regarding internal revenue department documents.


OVERVIEW: The City argued that BOTA's conclusions were erroneous because the assessed transactions did not constitute "sales" as defined by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3602(c) (Supp. 1992). The court disagreed and held that the interdepartmental transfers clearly constituted an exchange of property for consideration to the taxable water department. This conclusion prevented Kan. Admin. Regs. 92-19-72(a) from being construed to violate Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3606(b)(2) (Supp. 1992) and being declared void. Because water department fees had to be paid, whether by the developer or the final customer, in order to establish or maintain water from the City, they were part of the total cost to the consumer under Kan. Admin. Regs. 92-19-46. As such, they were included within the selling price under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3602(g) (Supp. 1992) and were a part of gross receipts under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3603(a) (Supp. 1992), upon which sales tax was collected. Computer equipment used by the water department did not qualify for an exemption under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3606(b) (Supp. 1992). BOTA's determination that it was without jurisdiction to award attorney fees under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3268 was correct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed BOTA's decision.

 

332 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Martis


No. 88,085


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 267; 83 P.3d 1216; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 66



February 6, 2004, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Martis v. McKune, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 41719 (D. Kan., June 6, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentences affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) of premeditated first-degree murder, second-degree murder, attempted first-degree murder, and attempted second-degree murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court's limitation of his right to cross-examine a witness on her pending charge denied him due process rights. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant's cross-examination was thorough and limited only with respect to the witness's pending charge. Defendant was allowed to exploit all inconsistencies in her original statement compared to her trial testimony and was allowed to ask about several convictions she had for crimes of dishonesty. Although defendant was denied cross-examination on the pending charge, the witness appeared on the stand in an orange jump suit, which was traditional prison garb. The trial court reasoned that it would have left an unfair impression with the jury that the witness was testifying falsely because she had made a deal with the State, with no evidence to support such an inference, and with the stipulation of defendant that no deal had even been discussed with the witness. Therefore, the trial court did not err.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Huff


No. 89,037


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 195; 83 P.3d 206; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 32



January 30, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in State v. Huff, 31 Kan. App. 2d 717, 71 P.3d 1185 (2003). Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.
State v. Huff, 31 Kan. App. 2d 717, 71 P.3d 1185, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 596 (2003).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Court of Appeals affirmed; district court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for review from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Kansas) affirming defendant's sentence. The court granted review on the sole issue of whether statutory authority existed for the imposition of consecutive jail sentences for misdemeanor offenses.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to attempted robbery, felony possession of marijuana following a prior conviction, misdemeanor theft, and two counts of misdemeanor child endangerment. The trial court imposed consecutive 12-month jail sentences on each of the three misdemeanor offenses, to run consecutive to the primary offense of attempted robbery. Defendant appealed her sentence, arguing that the trial court did not have authority to impose consecutive jail sentences on her misdemeanor offenses. The lower appellate court ruled that the trial court had statutory authority to impose consecutive imprisonment sentences on the misdemeanor offenses under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4608(a). On further appeal, defendant argued that the lower appellate court failed to address the distinction between the use of the terms "confinement" and "imprisonment" in the statutes authorizing the punishment for misdemeanor and felony offenses. The court rejected defendant's argument, stating that imprisonment and confinement were one and the same and could be used interchangeably. The court affirmed, holding that § 21-4608(a) empowered the trial court to impose consecutive sentences in misdemeanor convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

334 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cordray


NO. 87,349


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 43; 82 P.3d 503; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 18



January 16, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in an unpublished decision filed March 14, 2003. Appeal from Lyon district court. JOHN O. SANDERSON, judge.
State v. Cordray, 65 P.3d 221, 2003 Kan. App. LEXIS 188 (Kan. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which affirmed his conviction for reckless second-degree murder, but reversed his convictions for attempted reckless second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant fired his gun several times in the direction of the victims' car after a confrontation regarding the victims' vandalism of defendant's property. Although defendant testified that he did not intend to hit anyone, one of the passengers died after receiving three gunshot wounds. At trial, the trial court did not provide the jury with a definition of reckless. The court held that the instructions for the two offenses, taken together, acceptably conveyed the meanings and degrees of recklessness to the jury. The phrase "extreme indifference to the value of human life" was not so vague as to be unconstitutionally void. The court rejected defendant's argument that he had a right to an all-or-nothing defense. The 1998 amendment to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3107 did not relieve trial courts of the obligation to instruct on lesser offenses. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support the murder conviction, because defendant fired into darkness in the direction of a vehicle that he knew was occupied. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting the pre-death photograph of the victim, which served to identify the victim and show the extent of his wounds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals. The court reversed the convictions of attempted reckless second-degree murder and otherwise affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Hebert


No. 88,084


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


277 Kan. 61; 82 P.3d 470; 2004 Kan. LEXIS 17



January 16, 2004, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Clay district court, MERYL D. WILSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of capital murder, aggravated battery against a law enforcement officer, criminal use of weapons, and inflicting death to a police dog. The jury was unable to reach a unanimous decision on a death sentence, and the Clay District Court (Kansas) sentenced defendant to consecutive sentences for each crime, including life in prison without the possibility of parole for 50 years for capital murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the district court erred by failing to suppress a videotape of his pre-Miranda and post-Miranda statements. The court affirmed the district court's judgment, stating that, absent coercion or improper tactics by the officers in obtaining the initial statements, there was no presumption of compulsion as to the subsequent post-Miranda statements. In applying the factors to be considered in determining whether the confession was voluntary, the court found that there was nothing to suggest that defendant's post-Miranda statements were involuntary. Additionally, the court held that the district court's error in failing to sustain an objection to the prosecutor's closing remark that premeditation did not take any amount of time was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, as there was no evidence that the prosecutor's remark was a deliberate misstatement of the law and there was overwhelming evidence of premeditation. The court also held that the admission of a photograph of the deputy in his uniform was relevant to prove identity and to prove that he was a law enforcement officer, both of which were elements that the prosecution had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Armstrong


Nos. 88,692, 88,835, 89,595, 88,899


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 819; 80 P.3d 378; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 701



December 12, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; JOHN ANDERSON, III, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Johnson District Court (Kansas) dismissed four criminal cases on the ground that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3764(d) (Supp. 2002), which defines unlawful removal of a theft detection device, was unconstitutionally vague. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The four defendants were charged because they were observed removing electronic sensor tags from various retail items in various retail stores. They persuaded two district court judges that "theft detection device" and "prior to purchase" and "removal" did not convey a definite meaning to persons of common intelligence. The district court held the statute therefore failed the vagueness test. Persons of common intelligence would have no trouble understanding the meaning of the ordinary words making up the phrase "theft detection device" to mean any item used for the purpose of theft detection at the time of the act alleged to be a violation. The ordinary words that make up the phrase "prior to purchase" were even less conducive to confusion. Finally, with regard to "removal," the ordinary meaning of "removal" was commonly understood to be movement that put space between two things or relocated one thing from one place to another. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3764(d) (Supp. 2002), properly construed, included a requirement that the accused possess a specific intent to facilitate a theft, which provided adequate safeguards against arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded.

In re Colo. Interstate Gas Co.


No. 89,494


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 672; 79 P.3d 770; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 612



November 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.
In re Colorado Interstate Gas Co., 270 Kan. 303, 14 P.3d 1099, 2000 Kan. LEXIS 977 (2000).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public utility sought review of a decision from appellee Board of Tax Appeals (Kansas), which conducted a valuation of the utility's property in Kansas for ad valorem taxation purposes.


OVERVIEW: The utility argued on appeal that the Board applied an incorrect standard of review, used an incorrect allocation methodology, and erroneously taxed intangible property. The utility also contended that Kan. Const. art. 11, § 1(b)(2), to the extent that it denied public utilities an exemption from inventory taxation while granting exemption to merchants and manufacturers, violated the utility's equal protection rights under the United States Constitution. The court found no error, observing that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 74-2438 (1996 Supp.) required the Board to apply a de novo standard in reviewing a valuation by the Kansas Department of Revenue Property Valuation Division (PVD) and that the Board had done so. A taxpayer had the burden under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a04 to show by a preponderance of the evidence that the PVD was wrong and that substantial competent evidence supported the value asserted by the taxpayer. Original cost was an acceptable method of allocation under § 79-5a04 as an alternative to the unit valuation process. Intangible property could be taxed if it created an enhanced value of tangible property. There was no Commerce Clause or Equal Protection Clause violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the valuation decision.

In re ANR Pipeline Co.


No. 89,578


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 702; 79 P.3d 751; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 613; 158 Oil & Gas Rep. 1165



November 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pipeline company sought review from a decision by appellee Kansas Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA) regarding the value of its interstate company or its unit value over a two-year period.


OVERVIEW: On review, the company contended its unit value was overvalued, because BOTA based its valuation upon a forecast of the company's future income; intangible assets not subject to tax in Kansas were included in the unit values, and BOTA's adoption of overall capitalization rates were not supported by the weight of evidence. The appellate court disagreed, finding that the evidence supported BOTA's determination of the company's income for the years in question, notwithstanding the Department of Revenue Property Valuation Division's (PVD) use of historical evidence of the company's income. BOTA was acting within its area of expertise, and its decision to adopt the PVD's income figures for the years in question was entitled to great weight and deference. Further, BOTA's decision with respect to the capitalization rates were in line with the interstate natural gas pipeline industry. BOTA's inclusion of intangibles in its valuation did not raise a Kansas constitutional issue, and BOTA's decision to include such intangibles was factually and legally supported in the record.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

339 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Martinez


No. 88,046


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 527; 78 P.3d 769; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 582



October 31, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 14, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Finney District Court; MICHAEL L. QUINT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered an Alford plea to one count of burglary in the Finney District Court (Kansas). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2511 was unconstitutional because a DNA sample was not reasonably related to the crime of burglary, and therefore, the statute violated his Fourth Amendment rights. The supreme court disagreed. The State had a special need for accurately solving future crimes and, thereby, protecting the safety and welfare of Kansas citizens. That special need was beyond the normal need for law enforcement, because it did not in and of itself detect criminal wrongdoing. A convicted person had a lessened expectation of privacy. The State authorities were not allowed to choose which offenders would provide samples, but were directed by the statute to collect and catalog samples from all persons convicted of the listed crimes. There was nothing random or arbitrary about the State's action in collecting the DNA samples. Thus, a warrant was unnecessary to protect citizens from random or arbitrary DNA collection under the statute. In addition, there was the potential for finding DNA evidence at burglary crime scenes, because the entry could have been achieved through breaking windows.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Cent. Ill. Pub. Servs. Co.


No. 89,432


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 612; 78 P.3d 419; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 593



October 31, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a ruling from the Board of Tax Appeals (Kansas), which granted a tangible personal property tax exemption to appellees, out-of-state owners of natural gas stored in Kansas, on the ground that the owners did not meet the definition of "public utilities" found in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a01 (2002 Supp.).


OVERVIEW: The owners sought and obtained an exemption in accordance with Kan. Const. art. 11, § 1, which exempted merchants' and manufacturers' inventories from Kansas property tax but denied public utilities the merchants' inventory exemption. The court affirmed the ruling that the owners' natural gas inventory stored in Kansas was merchants' inventory and that the owners were entitled to an exemption under the implementing statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-201m (1988 Supp.). Pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-1439(b)(2)(C), the term "public utilities," for taxation purposes, was to be defined according to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5a01 and amendments thereto. Under the definition found in § 79-5a01(a)(4), the owners were not public utilities because they did not transport, distribute, sell, trade, or otherwise dispose of natural gas within Kansas and were not in the business of storing gas in Kansas. The court further held that taxing the natural gas inventory of Kansas public utilities, but not the natural gas inventory of non-Kansas public utilities, did not violate the uniform and equal rate of assessment and taxation clause of Kan. Const. art. 11, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling that granted the tax exemption.

Blue Cross & Blue Shield of Kan., Inc. v. Praeger


No. 89,075


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 232; 75 P.3d 226; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 481



August 6, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; TERRY L. BULLOCK, judge.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the district court reversed, and order of the Commissioner of Insurance affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Kansas Commissioner of Insurance, sought review of a decision from the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which reversed the Commissioner's denial of an acquisition request by appellee health insurance companies.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner denied the request because the acquiring company intended to reduce the reserves of the acquired company. The proposed reserves would have met the minimum requirements of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 40-2c01. The trial court, without conducting a substantial evidence review, concluded pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 77-621(c)(4) that the Commissioner had incorrectly interpreted and applied the acquisition statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 40-3304. The court, in reversing the trial court, observed that simply meeting statutory minimum surplus requirements or premium rate requirements for an existing domestic company did not automatically equate to operating in the interest of the public, policyholders, or the insurance-buying public in the context of acquisition by a foreign company. It was reasonable for the Commissioner to be concerned about the impact on policyholders of an abrupt reduction in a company's long-time surplus. Moreover, the statutory standards provided sufficient guidance to comply with Kan. Const. art. 2, § 1 and did not result in an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to an administrative agency.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and affirmed the order of the Commissioner.

 

342 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Kessler


No. 87,904


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 202; 73 P.3d 761; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 479



August 1, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction proceeding at Kessler v. State, 2007 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 479 (Kan. Ct. App., Jan. 12, 2007)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Kessler v. Cline, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 245 (D. Kan., Jan. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; REBECCA L. PILSHAW, judge.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, count one sentence vacated and remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) of two counts of aggravated indecent liberties with a child. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his convictions. The supreme court disagreed. The victim testified at trial to two separate touchings occurring on two separate nights. The victim indicated at trial that defendant touched his penis by closing his fist and moving it back and forth and indicated that defendant touched his buttocks by waving his hand back and forth. The testimony of the victim at trial was sufficiently similar to his testimony at the preliminary examination and his statements during the taped interview. Thus, the victim's testimony was sufficient to satisfy the first element of both counts of aggravated indecent liberties. The victim testified, and it was not controverted, that he was eight years old at the time of the alleged touchings, thus satisfying the second element of both counts. There was evidence that the alleged touchings occurred between January 1, 1999, and July 31, 1999. Thus, there was also evidence that satisfied the third element of both counts. Therefore, the evidence was sufficient to sustain defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. The sentence was vacated and the case was remanded with directions.

 

343 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. Sebelius


No. 90,404


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 87; 72 P.3d 553; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 397



July 11, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Writ of mandamus granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Democratic Party and its nominee brought an action in mandamus and quo warranto seeking an interpretation of and challenging the constitutionality of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 19-504, 25-3902, and 25-3903 (2002 Supp.), the scheme that provided for filling vacancies in offices, in particular, a vacancy created by the resignation of a county treasurer. Respondents were the Republican Party, its nominee, and the governor.


OVERVIEW: The county treasurer was elected as a Democrat, but switched to the Republican Party before she resigned. Each party selected a candidate that it certified to the governor. The Democrats sued for an interpretation of the governing statutes and to have their candidate appointed. The court agreed that mandamus and quo warranto were appropriate means for resolving the matter. Because statutory interpretation decided the case, the court did not reach the constitutional challenge. The issue was, when an officeholder changed political affiliation during her term of office and then resigned, did "of the party" in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 25-3902 mean the party to which the officeholder belonged when elected or when the vacancy occurred. The Republicans' First Amendment freedom of association argument was unavailing. An individual's freedom to switch party affiliation was not in question, nor was it relevant to the correct interpretation of the statute in question. In the court's view, public policy comported with common sense to dictate that "of the party" meant the political party to which the officeholder belonged at the time of the preceding election for that office.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of mandamus and directed the governor to appoint the candidate chosen by the county Democratic Party.

 

344 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Santos-Garza


No. 87,681


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 27; 72 P.3d 560; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 399



July 11, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court, WILLIAM A. CLEAVER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Sentence vacated and case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In sentencing defendant after he pled guilty to aggravated battery, the Johnson District Court, Kansas, imposed an upward durational departure sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended that his upward durational departure sentence imposed under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4716 (Supp. 2000) was constitutionally impermissible even though, in a plea agreement, defendant stipulated that, had the case gone to trial, the jury would have found the crime had been committed with the aggravating factor of excessive brutality. The supreme court found that the severity level of the crime, combined with defendant's criminal history score of I, called for a presumptive sentence of 38-41-43 months in prison; the trial court followed the plea agreement and sentenced defendant to 48 months in prison. As defendant received an upward durational departure sentence pursuant to § 21-4716, a statutory sentencing scheme the supreme court had repeatedly held to be unconstitutional on its face in prior cases, the supreme court concluded that defendant's sentence had to be vacated.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for resentencing within the statutory range.

State v. Hobbs


No. 88,327


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


276 Kan. 44; 71 P.3d 1140; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 400



July 11, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 25, 2003. As Corrected September 18, 2003.
Post-conviction proceeding at Hobbs v. State, 2006 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 504 (Kan. Ct. App., Mar. 17, 2006)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Hobbs v. McKune, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 82252 (D. Kan., Nov. 8, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court; PHILIP L. SIEVE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's motions to suppress his blood test, to suppress his blood alcohol test, and for a mistrial were denied, the Wyandotte District Court, Kansas, convicted him of involuntary manslaughter, aggravated battery, and leaving the scene of an injury accident. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended, inter alia, that the trial court erred in denying his motions to suppress and for mistrial. The appellate court found that defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1001(f)(1) (Supp. 2000) had no application to the instant case as defendant's slurred and mumbled speech, bloodshot and watery eyes, the strong odor of alcohol, and evidence linking him with the operation of the vehicle that caused the collision constituted ample bases for probable cause to believe that he operated a vehicle under the influence of alcohol. Defendant failed to show that admission of his blood alcohol test was unreasonable; even assuming admission of the test was error, it was harmless. As the juror's short discussion with a witness was an innocent contact on a topic unrelated to the prosecution, a mistrial was not warranted. Admitting the single autopsy photograph of the head and shoulder of the victim was not an abuse of discretion. Although it was error for the trial court to disallow the coroner's report of death as it was an official court record, the error was harmless.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

346 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cooper


No. 87,141


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 823; 69 P.3d 559; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 293



May 30, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 19, 2003.
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Cooper, 2011 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 484 (Kan. Ct. App., July 1, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Reno district court; RICHARD J. ROME, judge.
Cooper v. State, 270 Kan. 897, 2000 Kan. LEXIS 864 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 1982, defendant was sentenced, inter alia, to 3 to 10 years imprisonment for indecent liberties with a child. In 1999, defendant filed for post-conviction relief, claiming he should have been convicted of aggravated incest, as the child was his stepson. The district court denied relief. The Kansas Court of Appeals reversed. Upon remand, defendant received a 3 to 10 year prison sentence for aggravated incest. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was resentenced, pursuant to the authority of Carmichael. The Kansas Supreme Court held in Carmichael that the proper remedy for the petitioner's rape conviction of his daughter was to vacate the sentence imposed for rape and resentence the petitioner for aggravated incest. Defendant argued, inter alia, that he was deprived of due process by the district court's sentencing him for the uncharged and untried offense of aggravated incest. The United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit determined that the Kansas state courts' application of the Carmichael remedy comported with due process. Quoting Apprendi, the Tenth Circuit stated the relevant inquiry to be whether the required finding exposed the defendant to a greater punishment than that authorized by the jury's guilty verdict. Here, application of the Carmichael remedy did not affect defendant's conviction, nor was he found guilty of and sentenced for an offense for which he was not charged and convicted. Due process under Apprendi was not violated, as defendant was resentenced within the allowable penalty range for aggravated incest, which did not result in a more severe sentence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed defendant's sentence.

 

347 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


City of Wichita v. Hackett


No. 88,325


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 848; 69 P.3d 621; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 296



May 30, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; BRYCE ABBOTT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The municipal court found defendant guilty of driving under the influence (DUI) in violation of Wichita City, Kan., Ordinance § 11.38.150; at the time of the violation, he was riding a bicycle. Defendant appealed and the District Court of Sedgwick County, Kansas, convicted him of DUI. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended, inter alia, that the city was prohibited from proscribing his operation of a bicycle while under the influence of alcohol. Contrary to defendant's contention, the supreme court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-2001 authorized the city to expand the state's definition of "vehicle." Further, Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 8-1567, -1485 did not expressly authorize the operation of bicycles by those under the influence of alcohol. They merely failed to proscribe it. Consequently, no conflict existed between the city's ordinance and the state's statutes; thus, the ordinance was valid. However, while operating a bicycle under the influence violated the city code, it was not a DUI under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-1567. Thus, such a conviction did not count for state sentencing purposes. Finally, the supreme court determined that the city's DUI ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague as it conveyed sufficient definite warning and fair notice as to the prohibited conduct in light of common understanding, and conveyed sufficient clarity to those who applied the ordinance standards to protect against arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

348 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


In re Cent. Kan. E.N.T. Assocs., P.A.


No. 89,030, No. 89,031


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 893; 69 P.3d 595; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 281



May 30, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Kansas Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA) held that appellant medical association's aircraft were not exempt from taxation under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-201k as both aircraft were rented for personal use and, therefore, not regularly used exclusively to earn income under the terms of the statute. The association's motion for reconsideration was denied. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the association contended that its two planes were exempt from property and ad valorem taxes pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. M 79-201k, arguing that an amendment to the statute provided that an owner of an aircraft could earn income by renting the aircraft without losing the entitlement to the exemption. Contrary to the arguments of the association, the supreme court determined that the issue in the instant case was not whether the planes were used exclusively to earn income; rather, the issue was whether the planes were actually and regularly used exclusively to earn income for the owner in the conduct of the owner's business or industry. The supreme court found that there were two simultaneous uses of the planes, for business and for rental income, one which fit the provisions of § 79-201k and the other which failed to meet the statute's requirements. Thus, the planes were not actually and regularly used exclusively to earn income for the association in the conduct of the association's business. As such, the BOTA's denial of the tax exemption was proper.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

349 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


General Bldg. Contrs., L.L.C. v. Bd. of Shawnee County Comm'rs


No. 89,029


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 525; 66 P.3d 873; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 191



April 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; CHARLES E. ANDREWS, JR., judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged a decision of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which denied their injunction petition and held that Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 19-101a (Supp. 2001), 19-3801 et seq., 19-4101 et seq. provided authority for appellee county's eminent domain action against the landowners. The landowners also challenged an appraiser's award concerning the value of their property. The appeal was transferred to the court.


OVERVIEW: The county sought by eminent domain to acquire the property of the landowners for purposes of industrial or economic development. The landowners sought an injunction, but the request was denied. An appraiser's report was filed and determined the compensation for the property in question. After the county paid the appraiser's award into the court, the amount was withdrawn. The court affirmed. It was not critical that the landowners withdrew the awards while continuing their appeal from the appraiser's award, as, under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 26-510(b), they were allowed to do so without prejudice to their right to appeal, which statute applied to their appeal in the injunction action. Although one of the landowners' buildings had been demolished and they withdrew the appraiser's award, the court did not find the injunction issue moot because of the general issue raised. The court agreed with the trial court's analysis and held that counties had and could exercise the power of eminent domain to acquire property for industrial or economic development purposes. The court found that the proceedings were valid in this case and the injunction was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

State v. Bethel


No. 87,989


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 456; 66 P.3d 840; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 196



April 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bethel v. Kansas, 540 U.S. 1006, 124 S. Ct. 531, 157 L. Ed. 2d 412, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 8240 (2003)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Bethel v. Rohling, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 6952 (D. Kan., Feb. 22, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Crawford district court; DONALD R. NOLAN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Crawford District Court, Kansas, convicted defendant of one count of capital murder and two counts of premeditated first degree murder. Defendant appealed the convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3220 violated due process of law by abolishing the insanity defense, which was a fundamental element of the criminal justice system. The instant court concluded that the insanity defense was a creature of the 19th century and was not so ingrained in the legal system to constitute a fundamental principle of law. The legislature did not abolish the insanity defense but redefined it. The statute did not violate defendant's right to due process. Regarding defendant's argument that the Kansas "mens rea approach" to insanity unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof to him on the issue of intent once the State offered proof of the other elements, there was no backing away from the requirement that the State prove beyond a reasonable doubt every element of the offense charged. Regarding defendant's argument that the trial court erred in failing to suppress his confession on the ground that he was actively delusional during the interview, there was substantial competent evidence supporting the conclusion that defendant's statements were not unknowing or involuntary due to his mental condition at the time of the interview.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

State v. Gordon


No. 86,624, No. 86,625


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 393; 66 P.3d 903; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 205



April 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in 30 Kan. App. 2d ___, 50 P.3d 100 (2002). Appeal from Johnson County district court, STEPHEN R. TATUM, judge.
State v. Gordon, 50 P.3d 100, 2002 Kan. App. LEXIS 656 (Kan. Ct. App., 2002).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals dismissing the appeals is reversed. Judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed a petition for review from the decision of the appellate court (Kansas), which held that the district court lacked jurisdiction to extend two defendants' probation and further concluded that the extension of the second defendant's probation without a hearing was contrary to law.


OVERVIEW: Two defendants' consolidated cases came before the court on petition for review. The appellate court, interpreting provisions of Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-4611 and 22-3716 (2002 Supp.), concluded that the district court lacked jurisdiction to extend the defendants' probations and further concluded that the extension of the second defendant's probation without a hearing was contrary to law. The court concluded that the district court retained jurisdiction under Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 21-4611 and 22-3716 to extend without hearings the defendants' probations. Therefore the court reversed the decision of the appellate court and affirmed the district court's decision. There was no constitutional right to a hearing under the provisions of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4611(c)(7), and defendants' argument that equal protection principles were violated when the probation was extended without a hearing under the provisions of § 21-4611(c)(7) was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and affirmed the district court's decision.

In re Appeal of Family of Eagles, Ltd.


No. 88,118


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 479; 66 P.3d 858; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 197



April 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Kansas Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Kansas Board of Tax Appeals granted judgment in favor of appellee corporation after finding that the corporation did not have a substantial nexus with the state of Kansas, as required by the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution, to justify imposition of the duty to collect and remit tax payments required under the Kansas Compensating Tax Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3701 et seq. Appellant Kansas Department of Revenue appealed.


OVERVIEW: The department argued that the corporation's contacts in Kansas created a substantial nexus for imposing collection requirements under the Kansas Compensating Tax Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3701 et seq., the board failed to make certain factual findings and other findings were not supported by substantial evidence, the board improperly shifted the burden of persuasion to the department, and reasonable grounds for abating the penalties imposed by the department did not exist. The appellate court found that, although the corporation's Kansas independent service representatives (ISR's) did not have defined territories limited to Kansas, the distinction was one without constitutional significance. The sale of product by a Kansas ISR to an out-of state consumer was not subject to the Kansas sales or use tax. There was no impediment imposed upon interstate commerce. The lack of defined sales territories did not negate the physical presence that created a substantial nexus with Kansas sufficient to justify requiring the corporation to collect the tax. The ISR's did sell to Kansas residents, which helped developed the corporation's Kansas market.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded on the penalty issue to allow the board to review the issue.

 

353 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boyd


No. 86,553


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 271; 64 P.3d 419; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 122



March 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals in State v. Boyd, 30 Kan. App. 2d __, 47 P.3d 419 (2002). Appeal from Sedgwick district court; REBECCA L. PILSHAW, judge.
State v. Boyd, 47 P.3d 419, 2002 Kan. App. LEXIS 541 (Kan. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the court of appeals reversed; judgment of the district court reversed; case remanded for new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of possession of crack cocaine. The Kansas Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying her motion to suppress evidence, as the evidence was the product of an illegal search. The supreme court agreed. After stopping a car, a police officer found no drugs on the driver and had no probable cause to believe illegal drugs were in the car when defendant was told by the officer to get out of the car. Thus, at that point, the officer did not have probable cause to search defendant or her purse. The officer had no right to order her to leave her purse in the car. The State conceded at oral argument that, if defendant would have been allowed to take her purse with her, the officer could not have lawfully searched her or her purse. The protection of the Fourth Amendment could not be defined at the discretion of a law enforcement officer. Where a passenger was told by a police officer to get out of a lawfully stopped vehicle and, in response to the officer's order to leave her purse in the vehicle, put the purse down and exited the vehicle, a subsequent search of the purse as part of a search of the vehicle violated the passenger's Fourth Amendment right against unreasonable search and seizure.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

354 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Maass


No. 87,918


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 328; 64 P.3d 382; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 124



March 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; BRYCE ABBOTT, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) of burglary and theft. The court ordered defendant to submit blood and saliva specimens to the Kansas Bureau of Investigation. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by ordering him to submit the samples, as Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-2511(a) (2001 Supp.) was unconstitutional. The supreme court disagreed. The government interest outweighed defendant's diminished right for privacy in his genetic markers, as such information provided law enforcement with a dramatic tool that could be used to accurately identify a criminal suspect attempting to conceal his identity. Section 21-2511 was constitutional, as the minimally intrusive nature of providing blood and saliva samples was significantly outweighed by the State's interest in establishing and maintaining a statewide automated DNA database to search, match, and store DNA records. Therefore, the trial court did not err by ordering defendant to submit samples.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

355 of 1638 DOCUMENTS


State v. Childs


No. 88,556


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 338; 64 P.3d 389; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 125



March 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from McPherson district court; RICHARD B. WALKER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the McPherson County District Court (Kansas) of one count of possession of methamphetamine and one count of misdemeanor possession of drug paraphernalia. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Contraband was seized during an after-hours warrantless search of a licensed club that was conducted pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-2613, a provision of the Kansas Club and Drinking Establishment Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. 41-2601 et seq. Law enforcement officers gained entry to the club by telling defendant, the manager, to move out of the way or he would be arrested for obstruction. Defendant contended that the search was improper, as the officers gained entry by coercion. On appeal, the supreme court found that defendant's action constituted a refusal, as contemplated by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 41-2613. However, the court interpreted § 41-2613 to provide the sanction of license revocation as the exclusive sanction for refusing entry to duly authorized officers. The officers did not have authority to arrest defendant for obstruction of official duty, and an officer's overcoming defendant's resistance by threatening to arrest him amounted to gaining entry on false pretenses. Thus, the officers' entry and inspection was not authorized under § 41-2613 and constituted an unlawful entry and search. Therefore, the district court erred in failing to grant defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

State v. Barnes


No. 88,842, No. 88,843


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 364; 64 P.3d 405; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 126



March 7, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Geary district court; LARRY BENGTSON, judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate cases, defendants were charged with possession of methamphetamine with intent to sell within 1,000 feet of a school zone in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4161(a), (d), and other drug offenses. Defendants moved for dismissal of the schoolyard offenses. The Geary District Court (Kansas) granted the motion and dismissed the schoolyard offenses. The State appealed and the matters were consolidated for appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were observed parked in a car, which was in an alley behind a residence. The police followed the car while it meandered through the city and did not stop the car until it passed through a school zone and pulled into a fast food restaurant. A search of the car resulted in recovery of marijuana, methamphetamine, hydrocodone, guns, and drug paraphernalia. The Kansas Supreme Court found that: (1) the legislature did not intend Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-4161(d) to apply to persons who were not apprehended within the school zone and whose uninterrupted passage in a vehicle through the school zone was fortuitous, and (2) the district court did not err in dismissing the schoolyard counts against the defendants under the unique facts of this case.


OUTCOME: The appeal was denied.

Bd. of County Comm'rs v. Nielander


No. 88,844


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


275 Kan. 257; 62 P.3d 247; 2003 Kan. LEXIS 33



January 31, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Lincoln district court; THOMAS M. TUGGLE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee board of county commissioners sought injunctive relief in the Lincoln District Court, Kansas, against appellants, the county sheriff and a deputy sheriff, prohibiting the sheriff from employing the deputy and from making any purchase in excess of $ 250 without prior board approval. The district court granted the relief requested and the sheriff and deputy appealed.


OVERVIEW: Twice the board discharged the deputy, and twice the sheriff continued his employment. The board also adopted a purchase policy limiting major sheriff's purchases without prior board approval. The district court ordered the deputy discharged, enjoined him from working or attempting to work for the county, and enjoined the sheriff from continuing his employment. In addition, the district court mandatorily enjoined the sheriff to comply with the purchase policy. On appeal the sheriff and deputy challenged the board's authority to terminate a deputy's employment and to require preapproval of expenditures by a sheriff. The appellate court held that board's actions constituted an attempt to change the sheriff's grant of authority under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 19-805(a) to appoint, promote, demote, and dismiss deputies, and that the board was prohibited under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 19-101a(a)(15) (2001 Supp.) from effecting such a change. Further, the court held that where a board of county commissioners approved a budget including necessary expenses, the sheriff could not be required to obtain advance approval for purchases within the limits of the approved budget regardless of the amount.


OUTCOME: The appellate court vacated the district court's injunction discharging the deputy, enjoining the deputy from working or attempting to work as a county employee, and enjoining the sheriff from continuing the deputy's employment, and reversed the district court and vacated its injunction prohibiting the sheriff from making any purchase in excess of $ 250 without prior board approval.
